2:32:2090:0:14:72:325:700:12:Times:12:Times:House Price Beliefs And Mortgage Leverage Choice
2:33:2138:14:14:72:426:686:12:Times:12:Times:Michael Bailey, Eduardo Dávila, Theresa Kuchler, and Johannes Stroebel
2:34:2208:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:NBER Working Paper No. 24091
2:35:2236:14:14:72:150:658:12:Times:12:Times:November 2017
2:36:2249:14:28:72:334:644:12:Times:12:Times:JEL No. D1,D14,E03,G0,G02,G11,G12,G21,R21,R30
2:37:2294:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:ABSTRACT
2:38:2302:28:14:72:546:588:12:Times:12:Times:We  study  the  relationship  between  homebuyers'  beliefs  about  future  house  price  changes  and 
2:39:2405:14:14:72:545:574:12:Times:12:Times:their mortgage  leverage  choices.  From  a  theoretical  perspective,  whether  more  pessimistic 
2:40:2503:14:14:72:546:560:12:Times:12:Times:homebuyers choose  more  or  less  leverage  is  ambiguous  and  depends  on  their  willingness  to 
2:41:2603:14:14:72:546:546:12:Times:12:Times:reduce  the  size  of  their housing  investment.  When  households  primarily  maximize  the  levered 
2:42:2705:14:14:72:546:532:12:Times:12:Times:return  of  their  property  investment, more  pessimistic  homebuyers  reduce  their  leverage  to 
2:43:2804:14:14:72:545:518:12:Times:12:Times:purchase  smaller  houses.  On  the  other  hand, when  considerations  such  as  family  size  pin  down 
2:44:2909:14:14:72:545:504:12:Times:12:Times:the desired property size, pessimistic homebuyers reduce their financial exposure to the housing 
2:45:3005:14:14:72:546:490:12:Times:12:Times:market  by  making  smaller  downpayments  to  buy  similarly-sized homes.  To  determine  which 
2:46:3101:14:14:72:546:476:12:Times:12:Times:scenario  better  describes  the  data,  we  empirically  investigate  the  cross-sectional relationship 
2:47:3205:14:14:72:545:462:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:between  beliefs  and  leverage  choices  in  the  U.S.  housing  market.  Our  data  combine  mortgage 
2:48:3308:14:14:72:546:448:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:financing information and a housing market expectations survey with anonymized social network 
2:49:3401:14:14:72:546:434:12:Times:12:Times:data from Facebook. The survey shows that an individual's belief distribution about future house 
2:50:3498:14:14:72:546:420:12:Times:12:Times:price changes  is  affected  by  the  recent  house  price  experiences  of  her  geographically  distant 
2:51:3603:14:14:72:545:406:12:Times:12:Times:friends,  allowing us  to  use  these  experiences  as  quasi-exogenous  shifters  of  individuals'  house 
2:52:3709:14:14:72:546:392:12:Times:12:Times:price beliefs. We find that more pessimistic homebuyers make smaller downpayments and choose 
2:53:3801:14:14:72:546:378:12:Times:12:Times:higher  leverage,  in  particular in  states  where  default  costs  are  relatively  low,  as  well  as  during 
2:54:3913:14:14:72:545:364:12:Times:12:Times:periods when house prices are expected to fall on average. Overall, our results provide evidence 
2:55:4009:14:14:72:542:350:12:Times:12:Times:for  an  important  role  of  heterogeneous  beliefs in  explaining  individuals'  financial  decision-
2:56:4111:14:38:72:111:336:12:Times:12:Times:making.

2:73:4380:14:28:285:400:228:12:Times:12:Times:tkuchler@stern.nyu.edu
2:74:4402:28:14:285:372:200:12:Times:12:Times:Johannes Stroebel
2:75:4419:14:14:285:405:186:12:Times:12:Times:Stern School of Business
2:76:4443:14:14:285:389:172:12:Times:12:Times:New York University
2:77:4462:14:14:285:374:158:12:Times:12:Times:44 West 4th Street
2:78:4480:14:14:285:392:144:12:Times:12:Times:New York, NY 10012
2:79:4498:14:14:285:337:130:12:Times:12:Times:and NBER
2:80:4506:14:0:285:418:116:12:Times:12:Times:johannes.stroebel@nyu.edu
3:81:4531:0:15:75:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Aggregate leverage ratios in the economy vary substantially over time and play a key role in driving economic
3:82:4640:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:fluctuations (see Krishnamurthy and Muir, 2016; Mian, Sufi and Verner, 2016). Understanding the determinants of
3:83:4751:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:investors’ leverage choices is therefore a question of central economic importance. Leverage choices in the housing
3:84:4866:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:market are of particular interest to policy makers (see DeFusco, Johnson and Mondragon, 2017): mortgages are
3:85:4974:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the primary liability on households’ balance sheets, and increased household leverage plays a key role in many
3:86:5084:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:accounts of the recent Financial Crisis (e.g., Mian and Sufi, 2009). In this paper, we explore the role of homebuyers’
3:87:5202:16:15:60:484:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:beliefs about house price growth as one potential determinant of their mortgage leverage choices.
3:88:5299:15:16:75:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We first develop a parsimonious model that characterizes the channels through which homebuyers’ beliefs
3:89:5402:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:about future house price growth can affect their mortgage leverage choices. We show that the direction of the
3:90:5511:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:combined effect of beliefs on leverage choices is ambiguous, and depends on the willingness of relatively pessimistic
3:91:5628:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:homebuyers to reduce the size of their housing market investment by either renting or buying a smaller house in a
3:92:5741:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:cheaper neighborhood. We then empirically investigate the relationship between homebuyers’ beliefs about future
3:93:5852:16:15:60:552:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house price growth and their leverage choices, and find that more pessimistic homebuyers take on more leverage.
3:94:5963:15:16:75:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our model adapts the portfolio choice framework of Geanakoplos (2010) and Simsek (2013) to the housing
3:95:6065:16:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:market. We consider individuals who optimally choose non-housing consumption in addition to home size and
3:96:6170:16:15:60:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:mortgage leverage given a schedule of loan-to-value ratios and interest rates offered by lenders.  Introducing
3:97:6280:15:16:60:553:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:consumption as an additional choice margin allows households to separately determine the size of their home and
3:98:6391:16:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:their leverage. House price beliefs affect leverage choices through two forces. The first force works through the
3:99:6504:16:15:60:553:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:perceived expected return on housing investments (“expected return force”). More optimistic agents want to buy
3:100:6614:15:16:60:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:larger houses, since they expect each dollar invested in housing to earn a higher return. In order to afford the larger
3:101:6733:16:16:60:553:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:home, they need to further lever up their fixed resources. The second force works through the protection against
3:102:6845:16:15:60:553:400:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house price declines offered by the ability to default on mortgages (“downpayment protection force”).1Holding
3:103:6954:15:16:60:553:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house size fixed, more pessimistic agents, who perceive a higher probability of defaulting on their mortgage, choose
3:104:7070:16:16:60:520:369:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f3:lower downpayments and higher leverage to limit the potential loss of their own funds in case of default.2
3:105:7176:16:15:75:553:353:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our model captures an additional important feature of housing markets.  Since owner-occupied housing is
3:106:7279:15:16:60:553:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:both an investment and a consumption good, the optimal home is not just determined by homebuyers’ investment
3:107:7387:16:16:60:553:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:motives, but also by consumption-driven motives such as family size and neighborhood preferences. This introduces
3:108:7500:16:15:60:554:306:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:a“collateral adjustment friction”that reduces homebuyers’ willingness to adjust their property size in response to
3:109:7614:15:16:60:553:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:changes in house price beliefs.3For example, a large collateral adjustment friction could be the result of pessimists
3:110:7731:16:16:60:553:275:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:not being willing to purchase in a less-safe neighborhood, or not wanting to buy a much smaller house. Our main
3:111:7842:16:15:60:553:259:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:theoretical result is to show that the strength of this friction is central to determining the relationship between
3:112:7957:15:16:60:358:244:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:homebuyers’ house price beliefs and their mortgage leverage choices.
3:113:8025:16:16:75:553:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We highlight the key mechanisms in our model by considering two polar scenarios for which we can obtain
3:114:8128:16:14:60:554:212:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:explicit analytical results.  We first consider ahousing-as-investmentscenario in which there is no collateral
3:115:8238:14:13:71:553:198:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:1We show that this force is active independent of whether mortgage default is primarily strategic, with homeowners defaulting
3:116:8363:13:9:60:553:185:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:as soon as their home is sufficiently “underwater,” or whether mortgage default only occurs when households also receive a negative
3:117:8494:9:7:60:413:176:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:income shock that makes it impossible for them to continue their monthly mortgage payments.
3:118:8585:7:12:71:553:169:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:2As will become evident below, an equivalent exposition of the second force describes pessimists as perceiving a lower marginal
3:119:8712:12:7:60:537:157:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:cost of borrowing, since they expect to repay their loan in fewer states of the world. This makes higher leverage more appealing.
3:120:8841:7:12:71:553:150:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:3We use the term friction to capture any force that reduces the willingness of individuals to adjust their exposure to the housing
3:121:8971:12:9:60:553:138:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:collateral for pure investment motives. In our setting, this force comes from the preferences of homebuyers, who view their house not
3:122:9104:9:10:60:553:129:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:just as an investment, but also as a place to live, which puts additional constraints on house size. Our discussion of the collateral
3:123:9237:10:9:60:553:119:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:adjustment friction focuses on the intensive margin of housing investment. In housing markets, renting rather than buying can give
3:124:9367:9:10:60:553:110:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:individuals the option to separate their decision of what house to live in from the decision of the size of their housing market investment.
3:125:9507:10:9:60:553:100:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:However, in many cases, the set of properties available for rent is very different from the set of properties available for sale; indeed, large
3:126:9650:9:10:60:553:91:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:single-family residences are almost exclusively owner-occupied. Therefore, reducing one’s housing market exposure through renting
3:127:9779:10:9:60:553:81:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:will usually also involve a potentially costly adjustment to the type of housing that can be consumed. This force will have the same
3:128:9911:9:0:60:398:72:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:effect as the collateral adjustment friction we consider for the intensive margin adjustment.
4:129:10005:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:adjustment friction and property size and leverage are determined by both the expected return force and the
4:130:10112:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:downpayment protection force. Under the appropriate definition of optimism, this scenario recovers the predictions
4:131:10226:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:from the portfolio choice problem in Simsek (2013): more pessimistic homebuyers reduce their leverage to purchase
4:132:10339:16:15:60:476:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:smaller houses because the expected return force dominates the downpayment protection force.
4:133:10431:15:16:75:554:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We then consider ahousing-as-consumptionscenario, in which the size of the housing investment is completely
4:134:10538:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:pinned down by factors such as family size, and households allocate their resources between consumption and
4:135:10645:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:making a downpayment to purchase the house.  In this scenario, which corresponds to an infinite collateral
4:136:10751:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:adjustment friction, the expected return force is inactive, but the downpayment protection force continues to
4:137:10860:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:operate. As a result, more pessimistic agents make smaller downpayments to protect their assets against losses
4:138:10970:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in case of house price declines. In particular, a pessimistic homebuyer might think: “I need at least a 3-bedroom
4:139:11083:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house given my family size, but I think house prices are likely to fall, so I do not want to invest much of my own
4:140:11197:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:money.” Such a borrower would then naturally choose higher leverage. As a reader explained to the Washington
4:141:11305:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Post (2011): “An acquaintance once told me he doesn’t believe in putting much money down on a mortgage loan
4:142:11412:15:16:60:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:because that way he can ‘just walk away’ if things ever get bad. And walk away he did. His property is now in
4:143:11521:16:16:60:551:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:foreclosure.” In the paper, we provide extensive evidence for the prevalence of such thinking among homebuyers.
4:144:11632:16:15:75:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:When we move away from these polar cases, whether more pessimistic agents take on more or less leverage
4:145:11735:15:16:60:553:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:depends on the magnitude of the collateral adjustment friction that captures the extent to which optimal home
4:146:11844:16:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:size is determined by investment or consumption motives. The empirical contribution of this paper is to investigate
4:147:11959:16:15:60:553:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:which of these forces dominates in practice. This analysis faces a number of challenges. Testing the implications of
4:148:12075:15:16:60:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:models with belief heterogeneity is difficult because individuals’ beliefs are high-dimensional and hard-to-observe
4:149:12190:16:16:60:553:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:objects. In addition, even if we could cleanly elicit homebuyers’ house price beliefs, we rarely observe forces that
4:150:12306:16:15:60:553:400:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:induce heterogeneity in these beliefs without also inducing variation in their economic resources or other variables
4:151:12422:15:16:60:296:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that might independently affect their leverage choices.
4:152:12477:16:16:75:553:369:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our empirical approach builds on Bailey et al. (2017a), who document that the recent house price experiences
4:153:12585:16:15:60:553:353:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in an individual’s geographically distant social network affect her beliefs about the attractiveness of local housing
4:154:12702:15:16:60:553:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:market investments. Indeed, we verify that the recent house price experiences of geographically distant friends
4:155:12813:16:16:60:553:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:can be used as shifters of individuals’ beliefs about the distribution of expected future house price changes. We
4:156:12926:16:15:60:553:306:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:also show that friends’ house price experiences are unlikely to affect leverage choices through channels other than
4:157:13041:15:16:60:553:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:beliefs. This allows us to explore the empirical relationship between beliefs and leverage by analyzing the cross-
4:158:13155:16:16:60:553:275:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:sectional relationship between the leverage choices of individuals borrowing from the same lender to purchase
4:159:13264:16:15:60:553:259:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:homes at the same time and in the same neighborhood, and the house price experiences of these homebuyers’
4:160:13369:15:16:60:191:244:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:geographically distant friends.
4:161:13400:16:16:75:553:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In our data, we observe an anonymized snapshot of U.S. individuals’ friendship networks on Facebook, the
4:162:13504:16:15:60:553:212:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:largest online social network, with over 231 million users in the U.S. and Canada. Our first empirical step combines
4:163:13620:15:16:60:553:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:these data with responses to a housing market expectation survey that was conducted by Facebook in April 2017.
4:164:13730:16:16:60:553:181:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The survey, which targeted Facebook users in Los Angeles through their News Feeds, elicited a distribution
4:165:13836:16:15:60:553:165:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of respondents’ expectations for future house price changes in their own zip codes.  Individuals whose friends
4:166:13946:15:16:60:553:150:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:experienced more recent house price increases expect higher average future house price growth. Quantitatively, a
4:167:14058:16:16:60:553:134:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:one-percentage-point higher house price appreciation among an individual’s friends over the previous 24 months
4:168:14168:16:15:60:553:118:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:is associated with individuals expecting a 33 basis points higher house price growth over the subsequent year. We
4:169:14281:15:16:60:553:103:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:also find that individuals whose friends experienced more heterogeneous recent house price changes report a more
4:170:14393:16:16:60:553:87:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:dispersed distribution of expected future house price growth. This suggests that the house price experiences within
4:171:14508:16:0:60:553:71:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:an individual’s social network do not just affect her beliefs on average, but that other moments of the distribution
5:172:14625:0:15:60:419:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of friends’ experiences affect the corresponding moments of the belief distribution.
5:173:14709:15:16:75:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:To study the role of house price beliefs in shaping mortgage leverage choice, we match anonymized social
5:174:14813:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:network data on Facebook users from 2,900 U.S. zip codes to public record housing deeds data, which contain
5:175:14920:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:information on both transaction prices and mortgage choices. We observe information on homebuyers’ leverage
5:176:15027:15:16:60:388:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choices for about 1.35 million housing transactions between 2008 and 2014.
5:177:15101:16:16:75:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We first test the predictions of our model under the assumption that beliefs over future house price changes are
5:178:15213:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:normally distributed.4We find that individuals whose friends have experienced lower recent house price growth,
5:179:15323:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and who are thus more pessimistic about future house price growth, take on more leverage. Quantitatively, a one-
5:180:15435:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:percentage-point decrease in the average house price experiences among an individual’s friends over the prior two
5:181:15548:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:years is associated with an increase in the loan-to-value ratio of about 9 basis points. Combined with the estimates
5:182:15664:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:from the survey, this suggests that a one-percentage-point decrease in the expected house price appreciation over
5:183:15777:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the next twelve months leads individuals to increase their loan-to-value ratio by 26 basis points. These findings are
5:184:15894:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:aligned with the predictions of the housing-as-consumption scenario, and suggest a sizable collateral adjustment
5:185:16006:15:16:60:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friction in the choice of financing owner-occupied housing. Consistent with predictions from this scenario, we also
5:186:16121:16:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:find that a higher cross-sectional variance of friends’ house price experiences is associated with choosing higher
5:187:16235:16:15:60:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:leverage: holding mean beliefs fixed, individuals who have a more dispersed belief distribution perceive a higher
5:188:16348:15:16:60:318:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:probability of default, and thus reduce their downpayment.
5:189:16406:16:16:75:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We test a number of additional predictions from the model about the relationship between beliefs and leverage
5:190:16515:16:15:60:553:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choice in the housing-as-consumption scenario. First, the model suggests that the effects of beliefs on leverage
5:191:16627:15:16:60:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choices should be particularly large when the cost of default is relatively small, so that household would consider
5:192:16742:16:16:60:553:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:defaulting in response to declining house prices a less costly proposition.  Consistent with this, we find that
5:193:16853:16:15:60:553:400:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:more pessimistic individuals in non-recourse states, where lenders cannot recover losses on a mortgage by going
5:194:16964:15:16:60:553:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:after borrowers’ non-housing assets, are more likely to reduce their downpayments than pessimistic individuals in
5:195:17077:16:16:60:553:369:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:recourse states, where the ability of lenders to go after non-housing assets translates into a higher cost of default.
5:196:17195:16:15:75:553:353:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Second, in the housing-as-consumption scenario, changes in beliefs have larger effects on leverage choices
5:197:17301:15:16:60:553:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:for individuals that are relatively pessimistic to begin with. This is because, in this scenario, the key driver of
5:198:17416:16:16:60:553:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:differences in leverage choice is disagreement about the probability of default. Following large house price increases,
5:199:17535:16:15:60:553:306:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:even relatively pessimistic individuals assign small probabilities to default states of the world. The quantitative
5:200:17650:15:16:60:553:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:effects of cross-sectional differences in beliefs on optimal leverage are therefore relatively small. Consistent with
5:201:17767:16:16:60:553:275:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:this, we find the magnitude of the relationship between house price beliefs and leverage choice to be the strongest
5:202:17882:16:15:60:494:259:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:among individuals who are relatively more pessimistic and during periods of declining house prices.
5:203:17981:15:16:75:553:244:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Third, the relative strength of the downpayment protection force compared to the expected return force
5:204:18083:16:16:60:553:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:should be increasing in the collateral adjustment friction faced by homebuyers.  Consistent with this, we find
5:205:18193:16:15:60:553:212:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that pessimists reduce their downpayment more when buying homes in areas with a high homeownership rate,
5:206:18297:15:16:60:553:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:where they have fewer opportunities to reduce their exposure to the housing market by renting instead of buying.
5:207:18409:16:16:75:553:181:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Throughout the analysis, we argue that the house price experiences of a homebuyer’s geographically distant
5:208:18515:16:15:60:553:165:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friends affect her mortgage leverage choice only through influencing her beliefs about future house price changes.
5:209:18629:15:16:60:553:150:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We rule out other channels that could have explained the observed relationship. For example, we show that the
5:210:18738:16:16:60:553:134:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:results are not driven by wealth effects whereby a higher house price appreciation in areas where an individual has
5:211:18853:16:15:60:553:118:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friends increases her resources for making downpayments. Our results are also not explained by correlated shocks
5:212:18965:15:15:60:507:103:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to individuals and their friends, or by individuals learning about the cost of default from their friends.
5:213:19071:15:12:71:553:88:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:4By studying the case of normally distributed beliefs, we can derive unambiguous predictions for changes in the level and dispersion
5:214:19203:12:10:60:553:76:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of beliefs (the mean and standard deviation of beliefs about house price growth). Our non-parametric results highlight the importance
5:215:19336:10:0:60:519:66:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of using the appropriate definitions of optimism to generate unambiguous predictions about the effect of beliefs on leverage.
6:216:19462:0:15:75:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We provide ancillary for the mechanisms described above. First, we show that, in a correlational sense, the
6:217:19569:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:negative relationship between optimism and leverage can also be detected in the New York Fed Survey of Consumer
6:218:19680:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Expectations. Second, we conduct an additional survey on SurveyMonkey in which we presented 1,600 individuals
6:219:19789:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:with hypothetical house price scenarios, and asked them to recommend a mortgage option to a friend who has
6:220:19895:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:already decided on a particular house to buy. Survey respondents were more likely to recommend making smaller
6:221:20004:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:downpayments under scenarios with a higher probability of large house price declines, even though this entailed
6:222:20115:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:paying higher interest rates. When asked to explain their reasoning, many individuals described a thought process
6:223:20228:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:consistent with the downpayment protection force (e.g., “If he defaults and housing prices decrease so that he
6:224:20338:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:cannot sell to recoup his investment, he loses less deposit if he puts less down”).  We also document that a
6:225:20446:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:similar logic is often described on financial advice websites and blogs that discuss what downpayments individuals
6:226:20560:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:should make. We then show that many individuals do not consider mortgage default particularly costly, another
6:227:20669:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:requirement for a strong downpayment protection force. Lastly, we present evidence for a substantial collateral
6:228:20780:16:15:60:554:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:adjustment friction. In particular, we explore Zillow’sConsumer Housing Trends Report 2017, which highlights how
6:229:20892:15:16:60:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:consumption aspects such as location and home size are usually the most important determinants of individuals’
6:230:21002:16:16:60:409:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:property choice, and that potential investment returns are much less important.
6:231:21081:16:15:75:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Overall, our evidence points to an important role for house price beliefs in determining individuals’ mortgage
6:232:21191:15:16:60:553:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:leverage choices. In the presence of sizable collateral adjustment frictions, more pessimistic individuals do not
6:233:21304:16:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:buy substantially smaller houses, but instead reduce their exposure to the housing market by making a smaller
6:234:21413:16:15:60:422:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:downpayment, particularly when the costs of mortgage default are relatively small.
6:235:21495:15:16:75:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our paper contributes to the theoretical literature on collateralized credit with heterogeneous beliefs developed
6:236:21608:16:16:60:553:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:by Geanakoplos (1997, 2003, 2010) and Fostel and Geanakoplos (2008, 2012, 2015, 2016).5Within that literature,
6:237:21718:16:15:60:553:400:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:which has focused on environments in which investors exclusively make levered purchases of risky assets, our
6:238:21826:15:16:60:553:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:model is most closely related to the baseline environment studied by Simsek (2013).  We show that the same
6:239:21932:16:16:60:553:369:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:theoretical framework can deliver new predictions when (i) an additional margin of adjustment — a consumption-
6:240:22042:16:15:60:553:353:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:savings decision — is introduced, and (ii) homebuyers’ housing choices are not exclusively driven by the pecuniary
6:241:22156:15:16:60:553:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:investment return on housing. We operationalize the theory by developing implementable tests of how borrowers’
6:242:22266:16:16:60:553:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:beliefs affect their leverage choices. Our results provide support for theories in which investors’ beliefs are an
6:243:22380:16:15:60:553:306:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:important determinant of individual and aggregate behavior, and are complementary to the findings of Koudijs
6:244:22488:15:16:60:493:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and Voth (2016), who document the positive relation between lenders’ optimism and high leverage.
6:245:22584:16:16:75:553:275:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our theoretical observation that the relationship between borrowers’ beliefs and leverage choices depends on the
6:246:22696:16:15:60:553:259:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:magnitude of the collateral adjustment friction is likely to be important across a number of other settings. In the
6:247:22811:15:16:60:553:244:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:housing market, the consumption aspect of housing reduces households’ desire to adjust the size of their housing
6:248:22923:16:16:60:553:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:market investment based on their beliefs about future house price growth.  In a similar way, firms borrowing
6:249:23031:16:15:60:553:212:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:against their machinery might not be able to sell any collateral that remains important in the current production
6:250:23144:15:16:60:553:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:process, even if the firms expect the collateral to lose value in the future. Even for pure investment assets, such as
6:251:23262:16:16:60:553:181:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:stock holdings, an investor’s ability to dispose of those assets quickly might be affected by market liquidity, which
6:252:23379:16:15:60:553:165:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:could also introduce a collateral adjustment friction. In all of these settings, our model predicts that pessimistic
6:253:23495:15:16:60:553:150:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:agents would engage in significant (non-recourse) collateralized borrowing against any asset that they are unable
6:254:23608:16:16:60:554:134:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to sell. To our knowledge, we are the first toidentifythe ability or willingness to adjust collateral positions as the
6:255:23726:16:15:60:553:118:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:key feature that determines the relation between borrowers’ beliefs and leverage choices, although the primitive
6:256:23838:15:15:60:337:103:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f3:forces driving our model are important in many environments.6
6:257:23899:15:12:71:553:88:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:5Other applications of collateral equilibrium include Araújo, Schommer and Woodford (2015), Cao (2017), Gottardi and Kubler
6:258:24022:12:7:60:359:76:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:(2015), Geanakoplos and Zame (2014), Geerolf (2015) and He and Xiong (2012).
6:259:24098:7:0:71:553:69:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:6For example, the mechanism behind the downpayment protection force is also present in models of sovereign default, as those
7:260:24223:0:15:75:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:On the empirical side, we provide evidence for the importance of beliefs in determining housing and leverage
7:261:24331:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:decisions. We also show that leverage choice in the housing market is best described by a housing-as-consumption
7:262:24443:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:paradigm with a sizable collateral adjustment friction. This potentially sets it apart from other markets, such as
7:263:24557:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the stock market, where investment motives are the primary driving force determining the size of the investment.
7:264:24669:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our empirical findings also contribute to the growing literature that studies belief formation. Some recent work
7:265:24781:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:includes Malmendier and Nagel (2011, 2015), Greenwood and Shleifer (2014), and Kuchler and Zafar (2015). Much
7:266:24890:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of this literature has focused on explaining how individuals’ own experiences affect their expectations. We expand
7:267:25004:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:on the findings in Bailey et al. (2017a) to show that various moments of the experiences of individuals’ friends
7:268:25116:16:16:60:406:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:also affect the corresponding moments of those individuals’ belief distributions.
7:269:25197:16:15:75:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our results speak to the active debate about the extent to which the joint movement in house prices and
7:270:25300:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:mortgage leverage during the 2002-2006 housing boom period was the result of homebuyer optimism or other
7:271:25404:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:forces in the economy, such as credit supply shocks (Adelino, Schoar and Severino, 2016; Burnside, Eichenbaum
7:272:25513:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and Rebelo, 2016; Case, Shiller and Thompson, 2012; Cheng, Raina and Xiong, 2014; Di Maggio and Kermani, 2016;
7:273:25623:15:16:60:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Foote, Loewenstein and Willen, 2016; Glaeser, Gottlieb and Gyourko, 2012; Landvoigt, Piazzesi and Schneider,
7:274:25731:16:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:2015; Mian and Sufi, 2009; Nathanson and Zwick, 2014; Piazzesi and Schneider, 2009). Our findings suggest that,
7:275:25842:16:15:60:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:by itself, increased household optimism does not induce higher leverage, and that other forces, such as shifts in the
7:276:25959:15:16:60:554:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:credit supply function, also played an important role during the recent housing cycle.7This does not mean that
7:277:26069:16:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:homebuyer optimism was not important in explaining the boom-bust cycle inhouse prices. However, it does mean
7:278:26177:16:15:60:553:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that a singular focus on homebuyer beliefs would have counterfactually predicted a decline in mortgage leverage
7:279:26288:15:16:60:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:during this period.8More broadly, our findings provide support for a literature that explores the role of beliefs as
7:280:26404:16:16:60:553:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:drivers of credit and business cycles (see Gennaioli, Shleifer and Vishny, 2012; Xiong, 2012; Angeletos and Lian,
7:281:26517:16:38:60:344:400:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:2016; Gennaioli, Ma and Shleifer, 2016, for recent contributions).
7:282:26583:38:26:60:483:362:14:g_d0_f7:14:g_d0_f7:1  A Model of Leverage Choice with Heterogeneous Beliefs
7:283:26639:26:15:60:553:336:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In this section, we develop a parsimonious model of leverage choice that allows us to characterize the various
7:284:26749:15:33:60:438:321:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:channels through which homebuyers’ beliefs can affect their mortgage leverage choices.
7:285:26835:33:22:60:167:288:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:1.1  Environment
7:286:26851:22:15:60:554:266:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We consider an economy with two dates,t={0,1}, populated by two types of agents: borrowers, indexed byi,
7:287:26955:15:18:60:450:251:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and lenders, indexed byL.9There is a numeraire consumption good and a housing good.
7:288:27038:18:9:60:553:233:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:described in Aguiar and Amador (2013) and Uribe and Schmitt-Grohé (2017). For instance, a sovereign with pessimistic expectations
7:289:27167:9:10:60:553:224:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:about the performance of the economy anticipates the possibility of defaulting, which generates an incentive to borrow more. As
7:290:27294:10:9:60:553:214:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:described in Section 2.2.1, this effect is modulated by the costs of default, which are endogenous in the sovereign context, as emphasized
7:291:27432:9:7:60:372:205:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:by Bulow and Rogoff (1989) and Gennaioli, Martin and Rossi (2014), among others.
7:292:27512:7:12:71:553:198:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:7We show that, in both housing-as-consumption and housing-as-investment scenarios, lenders’ optimism can generate an increase
7:293:27637:12:10:60:553:186:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:in leverage. Intuitively, access to cheaper credit makes borrowers more willing to borrow. This result establishes that shifts in the
7:294:27770:10:9:60:75:176:8:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f5:distribution of borrowers’ and lenders’ beliefs have an asymmetric impact on equilibrium leverage.8
7:295:27869:9:10:75:553:167:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:This is consistent with the evidence in quantitative models of the housing market such as Kaplan, Mitman and Violante (2015),
7:296:27994:10:9:60:553:157:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:who show that more optimistic homebuyers can precipitate price increases, but that without a simultaneous relaxation in credit
7:297:28120:9:9:60:553:148:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:constraints, leverage will likely decrease. Such quantitative models incorporate general equilibrium effects of beliefs on house prices
7:298:28255:9:10:60:553:139:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:that our cross-sectional empirical strategy cannot speak to. For example, if more optimistic beliefs drive up house prices, this can
7:299:28387:10:9:60:553:129:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:provide an additional positive force on equilibrium leverage if the same downpayment to purchase the same house now involves taking
7:300:28518:9:10:60:553:120:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:on larger leverage. Quantitatively, the results in Kaplan, Mitman and Violante (2015) suggest that general equilibrium forces are not
7:301:28651:10:7:60:526:110:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:strong enough to overturn the negative correlation between homebuyer beliefs and leverage we uncovered in the cross section.
7:302:28775:7:12:71:553:103:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:9For simplicity, we present our results in a principal-agent formulation in which borrowers and lenders have predetermined roles.
7:303:28904:12:9:60:553:91:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:A common theme in existing work is that optimists endogenously become asset buyers and borrowers while pessimists become asset
7:304:29030:9:10:60:553:82:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:sellers and lenders. This type of sorting is natural if belief disagreement is the single source of heterogeneity. However, in housing
7:305:29164:10:9:60:553:72:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:markets, additional dimensions of heterogeneity (e.g., life-cycle motives, wealth, risk preferences, access to credit markets, etc.) also
7:306:29301:9:0:60:317:63:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:determine which agents become buyers and borrowers in equilibrium.

9:369:33614:22:15:60:554:525:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f2:Lenders.Lenders are risk neutral and perfectly competitive.  They require a predetermined rate of return
9:370:33718:15:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f2:1 +r, potentially different fromβ−1, and, for simplicity, have the ability to offer borrower-specific loan-to-value
9:371:33833:16:15:60:554:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:schedules.15In bankruptcy, lenders recover only a fractionη∈[0,1]of the value of the collateral. The remaining
9:372:33943:15:16:60:553:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:fraction1−ηcorresponds to bankruptcy costs.  Lenders’ perceptions over house price growth, which may be
9:373:34046:16:21:60:395:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:different from those of borrowers, are given by a distribution with cdfFL(·).
9:374:34123:21:16:60:553:442:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f2:Equilibrium.Anequilibriumis defined as consumption, borrowing, and housing choicesc0i,b0i,h0i, and default
9:375:34229:16:16:60:553:426:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:decisions by borrowers, such that borrowers maximize utility given the loan-to-value schedule offered by lenders
9:376:34341:16:15:60:553:410:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to break even. Since our goal is to derive testable cross-sectional predictions for changes in borrowers’ beliefs,
9:377:34455:15:16:60:553:395:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:we do not impose market clearing in the housing market. Fixing total housing supply and closing the housing
9:378:34562:16:15:60:553:379:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:market would not affect our cross-sectional predictions for leverage choices.  We work under the presumption
9:379:34670:15:16:60:553:364:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that homebuyers always find it optimal to borrow in equilibrium, and relegate a detailed discussion of regularity
9:380:34783:16:32:60:176:348:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:conditions to Appendix A.

9:405:36069:7:12:68:553:166:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:14While we model housing as an homogeneous good to maintain tractability, the collateral adjustment friction also proxies for
9:406:36194:12:10:60:553:154:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:households’ willingness to adjust their housing choice in other dimensions. For example, it does not just capture households’ willingness
9:407:36331:10:9:60:553:144:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:to buy a smaller house in the same neighborhood, but also their willingness to move to a cheaper neighborhood in other to reduce the
9:408:36463:9:7:60:232:135:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:fraction of their portfolio allocated to housing.
9:409:36512:7:12:68:553:128:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:15Our results remain valid under weaker assumptions on the determination of credit supply. For instance, our results are unchanged
9:410:36642:12:10:60:553:116:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:if lenders are restricted to offering a single loan-to-value schedule to all borrowers. A correct interpretation of our empirical findings
9:411:36780:10:9:60:553:106:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:requires that potentially unobserved characteristics used by lenders’ to offer loan-to-value schedules to borrowers must be orthogonal
9:412:36914:9:0:60:368:97:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:to the recent house price experiences of a borrower’s geographically distant friends.

10:431:37580:20:16:60:554:588:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f2:Remark. (Strategic default)There is a substantial literature that investigates the extent to which mortgage defaults
10:432:37696:16:15:60:553:572:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:represent strategic, ruthless defaults by households walking away from homes with negative equity (“underwater
10:433:37806:15:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:homes”), even though they have the resources to continue making mortgage payments (see Elul et al., 2010;
10:434:37911:16:16:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Bhutta, Dokko and Shan, 2017; Gerardi et al., 2017). This literature concludes that default usually happens at
10:435:38021:16:15:60:553:525:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:levels of negative equity that are larger than what would be predicted by a frictionless default model. Our model
10:436:38134:15:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:incorporates these findings through the size of the parameterκi, which captures other, non-financial default costs
10:437:38248:16:16:60:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(e.g., social stigma). As we discuss below, our theoretical and empirical findings do not depend on whether default
10:438:38363:16:15:60:553:478:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:is purely strategic or the result of also receiving negative income shocks. Indeed, our results only require that
10:439:38476:15:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:default ismore likelywhen house prices decline; in other words, the results are robust as long as negative equity
10:440:38589:16:21:60:337:447:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f3:is a necessary condition for default, even if it is not sufficient.16
10:441:38658:21:16:60:553:426:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f2:LTV schedules.Given borrowers’ default decisions, competitive lenders offer loan-to-value (LTV) schedules to

11:520:41600:64:16:75:554:424:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The optimality condition for consumption simply equates the marginal benefit of consumption, given byu′i(c0i),
11:521:41710:16:15:60:407:408:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:with the marginal cost of tightening the date-0budget constraint, given byλ0i.
11:522:41788:15:16:75:553:393:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The optimality condition for housing equates the marginal benefit of buying a larger house with the marginal
11:523:41896:16:16:60:553:377:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:cost of doing so. The marginal benefit of a housing investment is the net present value of the expected return
11:524:42006:16:15:60:553:361:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:received at date1. This is the first channel through which borrower beliefs affect leverage choice, referred to above
11:525:42123:15:16:60:553:346:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:as the “expected return” force. The marginal cost of the investment corresponds to the reduction in available
11:526:42232:16:16:60:553:330:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:resources at date0, valued by borrowers according toλ0i. When the housing constraint binds, borrowers’ housing
11:527:42342:16:15:60:553:314:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:corresponds toh0i=hiand Equation (6) simply definesν0i, the shadow value of relaxing the housing constraint.
11:528:42450:15:16:75:553:299:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The optimality condition for borrowing equates the marginal benefit of increasing the promised repayment,
11:529:42555:16:16:60:553:283:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:which corresponds to having more resources at date0, valued by borrowers asλ0i, with the marginal cost of doing
11:530:42666:16:15:60:553:267:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:so. The marginal cost corresponds to the net present value of the increased promised repayment in non-default
11:531:42775:15:16:60:554:252:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:states at date1. This is the second channel through which borrower beliefs matter for leverage, referred to above
11:532:42888:16:16:60:553:236:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:as the “downpayment protection” force. Intuitively, a pessimistic borrower that needs a house of a certain size but
11:533:43003:16:15:60:553:220:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:thinks that house prices might decrease is more inclined to choose a lower downpayment (higher repayment and
11:534:43111:15:16:60:553:205:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:higher LTV ratio), since the perceived probability of repaying the mortgage is low. Equivalently, an optimistic
11:535:43222:16:16:60:553:189:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:borrower with a similar house size target who thinks that house prices are likely to increase, expects to repay
11:536:43333:16:15:60:553:173:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:her mortgage in more states of the world. This increase in the perceived marginal cost of borrowing leads more
11:537:43443:15:16:60:450:158:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:optimistic borrowers to invest more of their own funds by making a larger downpayment.

11:542:44010:10:9:60:553:89:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:additional terms appear in Equation (7) to account for this effect, since borrowers make default decisions optimally.  Intuitively,

12:580:45399:16:16:60:553:525:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:will therefore make small downpayments and take on larger leverage. Alternatively, a dollar invested in housing

12:591:45665:32:23:60:240:460:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:1.3  Two alternative scenarios
12:592:45695:23:15:60:553:437:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:When borrowers have preferences for housing that are not purely driven by investment considerations and they
12:593:45803:15:16:60:553:422:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:can freely adjust their behavior along consumption, borrowing, and housing margins, it is not generally possible
12:594:45915:16:16:60:553:406:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to provide clear analytic characterizations of the effect of changes in beliefs on leverage choices.  However, by
12:595:46028:16:15:60:553:390:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:sequentially studying (i) a scenario with no collateral adjustment friction in which borrowers freely adjust their
12:596:46142:15:16:60:553:375:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:housing size, and (ii) a scenario in which borrowers’ housing size is fixed due to non-investment-related forces such
12:597:46259:16:16:60:553:359:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:as family (infinite collateral adjustment friction), we can provide comparative statics on the effects of beliefs shifts.
12:598:46380:16:15:75:553:343:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f4:We refer to the case in which borrowers freely adjust their housing choice as thehousing-as-investment
12:599:46482:15:16:60:553:328:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:scenario. We adopt this terminology to capture that borrowers’ housing decisions are mostly driven by investment
12:600:46594:16:15:60:553:312:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:considerations. In this case, borrowers make housing decisions purely based on its return as a levered investment
12:601:46707:15:16:60:553:297:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and there is no collateral adjustment friction.  We refer to the second case in which the housing decision is
12:602:46816:16:16:60:554:281:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:fixed as thehousing-as-consumptionscenario. We adopt this terminology to capture that idea that consumption
12:603:46923:16:15:60:553:265:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:considerations such as family Baileysize or locational preferences are mostly driving borrowers’ housing decision. In
12:604:47040:15:16:60:553:250:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:practice, we expect borrowers’ behavior to be a combination of the effects we identify under these polar scenarios.
12:605:47155:16:16:60:553:234:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The purpose of our empirical exercise is to determine which scenario more closely captures the dominant forces
12:606:47265:16:21:60:253:218:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:driving leverage in the U.S. housing market.

13:665:50440:7:12:68:553:124:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:20Throughout the paper, we compare belief distributions along dimensions that allow us to guarantee that truncated expectations and

13:668:50841:10:7:60:461:93:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:strategy only uses shifts in the distributionFi(·)and does not use information on actual default thresholds.

14:671:51202:0:25:60:453:729:14:g_d0_f7:14:g_d0_f7:2  Beliefs and Leverage Choice: Equilibrium Outcomes
14:672:51254:25:16:60:553:704:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We next study how changes in the distribution of borrowers’ beliefs affect equilibrium outcomes in the model
14:673:51362:16:16:60:553:688:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:described above. In the body of the paper, we impose a parametric assumption on the distribution of beliefs and
14:674:51473:16:15:60:553:672:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:develop comparative statics for changes in the key moments of the belief distribution. In Appendix B, we derive
14:675:51584:15:32:60:353:657:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and implement a non-parametric test of how beliefs affect leverage.
14:676:51651:32:23:60:227:625:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:2.1  Parametric Predictions
14:677:51678:23:15:60:553:602:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Throughout this section, we assume that borrowers’ beliefs about the expected growth rate of house prices follow
14:678:51790:15:29:60:369:587:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:a normal distribution with meanμiand standard deviationσi, that is,
14:679:51857:29:0:272:301:558:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:g∼iN
14:680:51861:0:8:304:309:566:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:(

14:682:51868:0:8:333:338:566:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
14:683:51869:8:29:339:342:558:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:.
14:684:51870:29:15:60:553:529:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We adopt the normal distribution for our parametric predictions based on the empirical evidence about the
14:685:51975:15:16:60:553:514:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:distribution of house price movements that we observe in our data. Appendix Figure A3 shows the distribution of
14:686:52086:16:16:60:553:498:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:annual house price changes since 1993 across U.S. counties, constructed using data from Zillow. The distribution is
14:687:52201:16:15:60:553:482:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:unimodal and approximately symmetric, which suggests that the normal distribution is a sensible parametric choice
14:688:52314:15:16:60:553:467:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(see also Panel (c) of Figure 4). When needed, we truncate the normal distribution atg= 0in all calculations.
14:689:52423:16:16:60:553:451:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:As we show below, one desirable feature of the normal distribution is that shifts in the two moments, mean and

14:691:52635:15:24:60:507:420:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:between beliefs and leverage in both the housing-as-investment and housing-as-consumption scenarios.

15:820:55843:0:7:392:397:195:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
15:821:55844:7:10:399:553:188:7:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f3:for a given threshold ̃δ−χi. The parameters
15:822:55887:10:11:60:554:178:7:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f3:used in Panel (a) areμ= 1.3,μ= 1.7, andσ= 0.2. The parameters used in Panel (b) areμ= 1.3,σ= 0.2, andσ= 0.4. In both panels, we
15:823:56014:11:29:60:121:167:7:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f3:set ̃δ−χi= 0.78.
15:824:56030:29:16:60:553:138:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:On the other hand, Equation (12) highlights that when a borrower finds it optimal not to adjust her housing
15:825:56137:16:15:60:553:122:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choice, perhaps because it is determined primarily by family size (housing-as-consumption scenario with an infinite
15:826:56252:15:16:60:553:107:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:collateral adjustment friction), leverage is decreasing in the perceived probability that the mortgage will be repaid,
15:827:56370:16:0:60:86:91:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f12:1−Fi
15:828:56374:0:8:88:98:99:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:( ̃
15:829:56377:8:0:93:119:91:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi
15:830:56381:0:8:119:124:99:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
15:831:56382:8:16:124:553:91:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:, for a given default threshold ̃δ−χi. In Figure 1, this is given by the probability-mass to the right
15:832:56484:16:15:60:553:75:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of the default threshold.  Panel (a) shows that a reduction in the average belief is perceived by borrowers as
15:833:56594:15:0:60:553:60:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:a reduction in the return to their downpayment, since now the resources put down as downpayment are more
16:834:56700:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:likely to be lost in case of default. In that case, more pessimistic borrowers optimally decide to make smaller
16:835:56811:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f12:downpayments and borrow more. In this scenario, when the probability of default is less than50%, a higherσi
16:836:56918:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:increases the probability of default (i.e., it reduces the probability mass to the right of the threshold), reducing the
16:837:57038:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:probability of repayment and reducing also the return on borrowers’ downpayment. Higher variance of expected
16:838:57146:15:16:60:378:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house price changes will thus induce borrowers to increase their leverage.
16:839:57220:16:16:75:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:It is worth highlighting that borrowers’ ability to default is crucial for our results.  Had we assumed that
16:840:57328:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:borrowers are always forced to repay (κi→∞), the downpayment protection force would be inactive, and changes
16:841:57436:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in borrowers’ beliefs would have no impact on leverage choice in the housing-as-consumption scenario. As discussed
16:842:57550:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:above, our theoretical and empirical results do not depend on whether default is purely strategic (i.e., sizable
16:843:57662:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:negative equity is a sufficient condition for default), or whether households only default if negative income shocks
16:844:57778:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:make it impossible for them to continue making their mortgage payments. This is because even in response to
16:845:57885:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:income shocks, households will only default when they have negative equity, and the mortgage is worth more
16:846:57991:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:than their house – if they had positive equity, they could just sell the house, repay the mortgage, and keep the
16:847:58103:15:16:60:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:difference. This means that in either scenario, mortgage default is more likely when house prices decline (negative
16:848:58218:16:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:equity is either sufficient, when default is strategic, or necessary, when default is in response to negative income
16:849:58334:16:32:60:274:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:shocks), which is what we require for our results.
16:850:58384:32:22:60:277:462:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:2.2  Additional Testable Predictions
16:851:58420:22:16:60:553:440:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The results in Proposition 1 establish that changes in beliefs can have differential implications for borrowers’
16:852:58532:16:15:60:553:424:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:leverage choices depending on the active margins of adjustment. In this section, we derive additional testable
16:853:58642:15:16:60:553:409:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:predictions from our theoretical framework. Specifically, we study how the relationship between beliefs and leverage
16:854:58758:16:16:60:553:393:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choices characterized in Proposition 1 varies with the magnitude of the default cost. We also analyze how this
16:855:58868:16:15:60:553:377:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:relationship varies with the average optimism of homebuyers.  As highlighted in the introduction, we find the
16:856:58977:15:16:60:553:362:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:empirical evidence to be more consistent with the housing-as-consumption scenario. In the interest of brevity, we
16:857:59090:16:16:60:553:346:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:therefore derive these additional predictions for the housing-as-consumption scenario in the body of the paper,
16:858:59201:16:29:60:553:330:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f3:and relegate the formal analysis of the empirically less-relevant housing-as-investment scenario to the Appendix.22
16:859:59316:29:22:60:257:301:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:2.2.1  Interaction with Cost of Default
16:860:59355:22:16:60:553:279:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We first derive testable predictions for how the ability with which lenders can seize borrowers’ collateral in case
16:861:59470:16:24:60:436:263:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of default affects the strength of the relation between belief shifts and leverage choices
16:862:59560:24:15:60:553:239:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f4:Proposition 2.(Interaction with cost of default.)In the housing-as-consumption scenario, a given increase in
16:863:59668:15:16:60:554:224:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:the average belief,μi, generates a smaller decrease in leverage when borrowers’ default costκiis higher, as long as
16:864:59783:16:16:60:554:208:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:the default probability is less than50%– the empirically appropriate case – for any default cost. A given increase
16:865:59897:16:15:60:554:192:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:in belief dispersion,σi,generates a smaller increase in leverage when borrowers’ default costκiis higher, as long
16:866:60010:15:16:60:554:177:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:as the default probability is less than16%– the empirically appropriate case – for any default cost. Hence, high
16:867:60122:16:15:60:553:161:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:default costs dampen the effects of belief changes on leverage. Formally, the predictions for equilibrium LTV ratios
16:868:60238:15:12:68:553:146:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:22Note that when characterizing Propositions 2 and 3 below, we exclusively focus on the direct effects of changes in the default cost
16:869:60371:12:9:60:553:134:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:and average beliefs. As we describe in the Appendix, second-order comparative statics exercises are determined by both direct effects
16:870:60504:9:0:60:292:125:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:and curvature effects. Our focus is on the former set of effects.
17:871:60571:0:25:60:146:729:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:can be expressed as:
17:872:60591:25:0:148:158:704:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f13:∂2
17:873:60593:0:8:160:165:712:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:(
17:874:60594:8:0:165:192:704:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f12:1−Fi
17:875:60598:0:8:194:204:712:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:( ̃
17:876:60601:8:0:198:224:704:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi
17:877:60605:0:22:225:234:712:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:))
17:878:60607:22:0:176:206:690:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂μi∂κi
17:879:60613:0:0:238:294:697:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f10:<0,∀ ̃δ⇒
17:880:60621:0:14:314:332:704:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂2δi
17:881:60625:14:0:308:338:690:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂μi∂κi
17:882:60631:0:22:342:437:697:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:<0,if ̃δ < μi+χi.
17:883:60648:22:0:148:158:675:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f13:∂2
17:884:60650:0:8:160:165:683:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:(
17:885:60651:8:0:165:192:675:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f12:1−Fi
17:886:60655:0:8:194:204:683:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:( ̃
17:887:60658:8:0:198:224:675:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi
17:888:60662:0:22:225:234:683:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:))
17:889:60664:22:0:176:206:661:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂σi∂κi
17:890:60670:0:0:238:294:668:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f10:>0,∀ ̃δ⇒
17:891:60678:0:14:314:331:675:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂2δi
17:892:60682:14:0:308:337:661:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂σi∂κi
17:893:60688:0:0:342:381:668:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f4:<0,if
17:894:60693:0:8:387:395:676:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:∣∣ ̃
17:895:60697:8:0:390:438:668:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi−μi
17:896:60704:0:8:438:441:676:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:∣∣
17:897:60706:8:29:444:467:668:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:< σi.
17:898:60711:29:15:60:553:639:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Proposition 2 shows that a high default cost dampens the strength of the downpayment protection force. Intuitively,
17:899:60826:15:16:60:553:624:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:when default costs are large, the probability of default is low, so a given change inμiandσishifts only a small
17:900:60937:16:16:60:553:608:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:mass of the belief distribution from the default to no-default region or vice versa. Alternatively, when default costs
17:901:61055:16:15:60:554:592:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:are low, borrowers perceive that default is relatively more likely, so a given change inμiandσishifts a larger mass
17:902:61170:15:16:60:553:577:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of the belief distribution from the default to no-default region or vice versa. In the empirical implementation, we
17:903:61285:16:30:60:548:561:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:will use differences across states in lenders’ recourse to borrowers’ non-housing assets to provide variation inκi.
17:904:61400:30:22:60:257:531:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:2.2.2  Interaction with Average Beliefs
17:905:61439:22:15:60:553:509:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We next use our framework to derive testable predictions regarding the relative importance of changes in beliefs
17:906:61551:15:24:60:487:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:on leverage choices across individuals or time periods with relatively higher or lower mean beliefs.
17:907:61651:24:16:60:553:470:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f4:Proposition 3.(Interaction with average beliefs.)In the housing-as-consumption scenario, a given increase in
17:908:61759:16:15:60:553:454:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:the average belief,μi, generates a smaller decrease in leverage when borrowers’ average belief,μi, is higher to begin
17:909:61876:15:16:60:554:439:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:with, as long as the default probability is less than50%– the empirically appropriate case – for any default cost.
17:910:61990:16:16:60:553:423:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:A given increase in belief dispersion,σi,generates a smaller increase in leverage when borrowers’ average belief,
17:911:62103:16:15:60:553:407:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f4:μi,is higher to begin with, as long as the default probability is less than16%– the empirically appropriate case –
17:912:62217:15:16:60:553:392:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:for any default cost. Hence, high average beliefs dampen the effects of belief changes on leverage. Formally, these
17:913:62332:16:25:60:315:376:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:predictions for equilibrium LTV ratios can be expressed as:
17:914:62391:25:0:148:158:351:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f13:∂2
17:915:62393:0:8:160:165:359:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:(
17:916:62394:8:0:165:191:351:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f12:1−Fi
17:917:62398:0:8:193:203:359:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:( ̃
17:918:62401:8:0:198:224:351:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi
17:919:62405:0:22:225:234:359:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:))
17:920:62407:22:0:183:198:337:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂μ2i
17:921:62411:0:0:238:313:344:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:<0,∀ ̃δ⇒∂
17:922:62420:0:17:314:325:354:7:g_d0_f13:7:g_d0_f12:2δi
17:923:62423:17:0:309:323:337:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂μ2i
17:924:62427:0:23:330:425:344:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:>0,if ̃δ < μi+χi.
17:925:62444:23:0:148:158:321:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f13:∂2
17:926:62446:0:8:160:165:329:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:(
17:927:62447:8:0:165:191:321:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f12:1−Fi
17:928:62451:0:8:193:203:329:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:( ̃
17:929:62454:8:0:198:224:321:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi
17:930:62458:0:22:225:234:329:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:))
17:931:62460:22:0:176:206:307:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂σi∂μi
17:932:62466:0:0:238:294:314:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f10:>0,∀ ̃δ⇒
17:933:62474:0:14:314:331:321:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂2δi
17:934:62478:14:0:308:337:307:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:∂σi∂μi
17:935:62484:0:0:342:381:314:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f4:<0,if
17:936:62489:0:8:387:395:322:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:∣∣ ̃
17:937:62493:8:0:390:438:314:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi−μi
17:938:62500:0:8:438:441:322:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:∣∣
17:939:62502:8:43:444:467:314:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:< σi.
17:940:62507:43:15:60:553:271:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Proposition 3 shows that in the housing-as-consumption scenario, the effect of changes inμiandσiare smaller
17:941:62614:15:16:60:554:256:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in magnitude whenμiis higher to begin with. Panel (a) of Figure 2 shows that the probability of repayment,
17:942:62720:16:0:60:87:240:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f12:1−Fi
17:943:62724:0:8:89:99:248:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:( ̃
17:944:62727:8:0:94:120:240:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi
17:945:62731:0:8:120:125:248:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
17:946:62732:8:16:125:554:240:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:, is concave inμi. Intuitively, a given increase in optimism changes the probability of repayment
17:947:62829:16:15:60:553:224:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:by less when the average expectation is already high and default is unlikely. Panel (b) of Figure 2 shows that the
17:948:62943:15:16:60:553:209:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:dashed line, which corresponds to the repayment probability as a function of the variance of beliefs when expected
17:949:63057:16:16:60:553:193:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house price growth is high, is flatter than the solid line, which corresponds to the repayment probability when
17:950:63168:16:15:60:554:177:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:expected high price growth is low. Intuitively, whenμiis low to begin with, a larger mass of the belief distribution
17:951:63284:15:0:60:350:162:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f12:is around the default threshold, increasing the sensitivity of1−Fi
17:952:63350:0:8:352:362:170:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f9:( ̃
17:953:63353:8:0:357:383:162:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f12:δ−χi
17:954:63357:0:8:383:388:170:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
17:955:63358:8:16:391:507:162:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to changes in the variance.
17:956:63385:16:16:75:553:146:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We will provide both cross-sectional and time-series evidence for Proposition 3. Cross-sectionally, we will show
17:957:63497:16:15:60:553:130:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:a stronger negative relation between beliefs and leverage for the most pessimistic individuals. In the time series,
17:958:63612:15:0:60:546:115:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:we document a stronger negative relation between beliefs and leverage during periods of declining house prices.
18:959:63725:0:20:135:480:736:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Figure 2: Interaction with average beliefs: Housing-as-consumption predictions
