18:977:64118:9:30:60:137:500:7:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f3:figures, we setχi= 0.
18:978:64139:30:22:60:230:470:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:2.3  Role of Lenders’ Beliefs
18:979:64168:22:16:60:553:448:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Although our empirical findings build on cross-sectional variation on homebuyers’ beliefs, it is valuable to separately
18:980:64287:16:15:60:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:characterize the effect of changes in lenders’ beliefs on the equilibrium leverage choices of borrowers. This allows
18:981:64403:15:16:60:553:417:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:us to draw implications of our cross-sectional results for aggregate variables, as we discuss in the conclusion. We
18:982:64518:16:16:60:553:401:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:show that an increase in lenders’ optimism can increase equilibrium leverage in both the housing-as-consumption
18:983:64629:16:21:60:554:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and the housing-as-investment scenarios. We assume that lenders have normally distributed beliefs with meanμL.
18:984:64739:21:16:60:553:364:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f4:Proposition 4.(Role of lenders’ beliefs.)In the housing-as-investment scenario and the housing-as-consumption
18:985:64848:16:16:60:553:348:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:scenario, higher optimism by lenders, in the form of a higher average beliefμL, holding all else constant, including
18:986:64964:16:22:60:349:332:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:borrowers’ beliefs, is necessary to generate an increase in leverage.
18:987:65033:22:15:75:553:310:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In Appendix A, we formally show that a change in the average belief of lendersμLis associated with point-
18:988:65138:15:16:60:554:295:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:wise shifts inΛi(·)andΛ′i(·). Intuitively, when lenders become more optimistic, borrowers have access to cheaper
18:989:65250:16:16:60:553:279:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:funding. In the housing-as-investment scenario, we show that access to cheaper credit increases the maximum
18:990:65357:16:15:60:553:263:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:levered return that a borrower can achieve, generating a substitution effect towards higher leverage in equilibrium.
18:991:65473:15:16:60:553:248:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In the housing-as-consumption scenario, in addition to the substitution effect, there is an income effect that works
18:992:65589:16:16:60:553:232:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in the opposite direction: lower interest rates make borrowers feel richer, which generates a force towards higher
18:993:65703:16:15:60:553:216:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:consumption and lower leverage.  The findings of Propositions 1 and 4 thus show that shifts in the beliefs of
18:994:65812:15:38:60:529:201:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:borrowers and lenders, when considered in isolation, can have opposite predictions for equilibrium leverage.
18:995:65920:38:26:60:459:163:14:g_d0_f7:14:g_d0_f7:3  Beliefs and Leverage Choice: Empirical Investigation
18:996:65975:26:15:60:553:137:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The previous sections showed that the effect of homebuyer beliefs on leverage choice is ambiguous. If households are
18:997:66091:15:16:60:553:122:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:able to substantially adjust the size of their property in response to changes in expected house price growth, then
18:998:66206:16:16:60:553:106:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:more pessimistic buyers will buy smaller houses with lower overall leverage. On the other hand, some homebuyers’
18:999:66318:16:15:60:553:90:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:property choices might be primarily determined by factors related to the consumption aspect of the house, such as
18:1000:66431:15:16:60:553:75:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:family size. Such a collateral adjustment friction limits the ability of pessimistic homebuyers to reduce their home
18:1001:66547:16:0:60:553:59:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:size, and the only way to minimize their financial exposure to the housing market is to reduce their downpayment.
19:1002:66662:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In practice, individuals’ behavior will be in between the extreme cases studied in Section 2.1. Indeed, whether
19:1003:66773:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:collateral adjustment frictions are sufficiently large such that more optimistic individuals will choose lower leverage
19:1004:66892:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:is an empirical question that is central to understanding the role of homebuyer beliefs in driving mortgage leverage.
19:1005:67009:16:15:75:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In this section, we analyze the effects on equilibrium leverage of changes in homebuyer beliefs, holding lender
19:1006:67120:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:beliefs fixed.  Our tests use the recent house price experiences of geographically distant friends as shifters of
19:1007:67233:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:individuals’ beliefs about the distribution of future house price changes. Specifically, we compare the leverage
19:1008:67345:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choices of otherwise similar individuals purchasing houses at the same point in time and in the same neighborhood,
19:1009:67459:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:where one individual’s friends have experienced more positive, or more widely dispersed recent house price growth.
19:1010:67573:16:16:75:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:This empirical approach builds on recent evidence in Bailey et al. (2017a), who show that the recent house price
19:1011:67685:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:changes experienced within an individual’s social network affect that individual’s beliefs about the attractiveness
19:1012:67800:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of local housing market investments. We expand on these findings, and analyze responses to a new survey that
19:1013:67908:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:elicits individuals’ distribution of beliefs about future house price changes.  We document that the mean and
19:1014:68017:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:standard deviation of the house price experiences across an individual’s friends shift the corresponding moments
19:1015:68129:15:16:60:554:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of the distribution of individuals’ house price beliefs. Bailey et al. (2017a) also show that there is no systematic
19:1016:68245:16:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:relationship between the house price experiences of an individual’s geographically distant friends and other
19:1017:68353:16:15:60:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:characteristics of that individual that might also affect her housing market investment behavior.  This means
19:1018:68462:15:16:60:553:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that the house price experiences of an individual’s geographically distant friends are likely to only affect leverage
19:1019:68579:16:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choice through their effects on beliefs. Indeed, in our empirical analysis we will rule out a number of other initially
19:1020:68698:16:32:60:141:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:plausible channels.
19:1021:68717:32:22:60:337:415:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:3.1  Data Description and Summary Statistics
19:1022:68761:22:16:60:553:393:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our empirical analysis is based on the combination of two key data sets. First, to measure different individuals’
19:1023:68874:16:15:60:553:377:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:social networks, we use anonymized social network data from Facebook. Facebook was created in 2004 as a college-
19:1024:68986:15:16:60:553:362:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:wide online social networking service for students to maintain a profile and communicate with their friends. It has
19:1025:69101:16:16:60:553:346:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:since grown to become the world’s largest online social networking service, with over 1.9 billion monthly active
19:1026:69213:16:15:60:553:330:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:users globally and 234 million monthly active users in the U.S. and Canada (Facebook, 2017). Our baseline data
19:1027:69323:15:16:60:553:315:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:include a de-identified snapshot of all U.S.-based active Facebook users from July 1, 2015. For these users, we
19:1028:69434:16:16:60:553:299:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:observe the county of residence as well as the set of other Facebook users they are connected to. Using the language
19:1029:69550:16:15:60:553:283:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:adopted by the Facebook community, we call these connections “friends.” Indeed, in the U.S., Facebook serves
19:1030:69658:15:16:60:553:268:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:primarily as a platform for real-world friends and acquaintances to interact online, and people usually only add
19:1031:69770:16:16:60:553:252:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:connections to individuals on Facebook whom they know in the real world (Hampton et al., 2011; Jones et al.,
19:1032:69878:16:15:60:176:236:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:2013; Bailey et al., 2017b).
19:1033:69906:15:16:75:553:221:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:To construct a measure of the house price experiences in different individuals’ social networks, we combine the
19:1034:70017:16:16:60:553:205:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:data on the county of residence of different individuals’ friends with county-level house price indices from Zillow.
19:1035:70133:16:15:60:553:189:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:As we describe below, this allows us to analyze the full distribution of recent house price experiences across any
19:1036:70247:15:16:60:553:174:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:group of Facebook users. For example, we can calculate the standard deviation of house price experiences across
19:1037:70358:16:16:60:275:158:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:every individuals’ out-of-commuting zone friends.
19:1038:70407:16:15:75:553:142:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In order to measure homebuyers’ leverage choice, we merge individuals on Facebook with three snapshots
19:1039:70509:15:16:60:553:127:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:from Acxiom InfoBase for July 2010, July 2012, and July 2014.23These data are collected by Acxiom, a leading
19:1040:70617:16:15:60:553:111:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:marketing services and analytics company, and contain a wide range of individual-level demographic information
19:1041:70727:15:12:68:553:96:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:23This merge involves a scrambled merge-key based on common characteristics. 53% of merges relied on email address. Other
19:1042:70848:12:9:60:553:84:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:characteristics were full date of birth (51%) or year-month of date of birth (28%), last name (45%) and first name (84%), location at
19:1043:70981:9:10:60:553:75:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:the level of zip code (44%), county (37%), Core Based Statistical Area (8%), and telephone number (2%). Most matches are based on
19:1044:71110:10:0:60:148:65:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:multiple characteristics.
20:1045:71137:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:compiled from a large number of sources (e.g., public records, surveys, and warranty registrations). We observe
20:1046:71248:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:information on age, marital status, education, occupation, income, household size, and homeownership status. For
20:1047:71360:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:current homeowners, the data also include information on housing transactions after 1993 that led to the ongoing
20:1048:71472:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:homeownership spell, compiled from public records deeds recordings. These data include the transaction date, the
20:1049:71584:15:16:60:428:667:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f3:transaction price, and details on any mortgage used to finance the home purchase.24
20:1050:71667:16:16:75:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Since we can only analyze origination mortgages that have not been refinanced by the time we observe the
20:1051:71771:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:transaction in an Acxiom snapshot, we focus our analysis on home purchases between 2008 and 2014. There are
20:1052:71878:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:at most two years between these transaction and the closest Acxiom snapshot, allowing us to observe virtually
20:1053:71987:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:all of the initial mortgages. We want to study the mortgage choices for home purchases across a dispersed set of
20:1054:72099:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:geographies, in order to provide us with cross-sectional heterogeneity in the cost of default created by differences
20:1055:72215:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in the legal environment across states. To achieve this, we first define a set of eligible geographies as those with
20:1056:72331:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:good Acxiom coverage and complete reporting of house prices and mortgage amounts; this excludes, for example,
20:1057:72440:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:property transactions from non-disclosure states such as Texas, where housing transaction prices are not reported
20:1058:72553:15:16:60:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in the public record. We select all transactions in our data since January 2008 from a random sample of 2,900 zip
20:1059:72666:16:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:codes from these eligible geographies. This provides us with about 1.35 million housing transactions for which we
20:1060:72779:16:15:60:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:can match the buyer to Facebook.25These transactions come from 33 different states, with the most prominently
20:1061:72888:15:16:60:553:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:represented states being California (24.6%), Florida (12.4%), and Arizona (9.5%), with Ohio, Washington, Nevada
20:1062:72999:16:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and North Carolina each contributing approximately 5% of all transactions. Figure 3 shows how these transactions
20:1063:73111:16:21:60:171:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:are distributed over time.
20:1064:73137:21:245:232:381:426:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Figure 3: Number of Transactions
20:1065:73241:245:29:60:475:181:8:g_d0_f30:8:g_d0_f6:Note: Figure shows the number of transactions by purchase year in our matched transaction-Facebook sample.
20:1066:73347:29:15:75:553:152:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Table 1 shows summary statistics for our sample.26The average combined loan-to-value (CLTV) ratio across
20:1067:73451:15:12:68:553:137:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:24While the original deeds data contain the precise information on mortgage amount and purchase price, the Acxiom data include
20:1068:73577:12:9:60:553:125:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:these in ranges of $50,000. We take the mid-point of the range as the transaction price and mortgage amount. While the resulting
20:1069:73705:9:10:60:553:116:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:measurement error in LTV ratios does not affect our ability to obtain unbiased estimates in regressions where LTV ratio is the
20:1070:73831:10:9:60:75:106:8:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f5:dependent variable, it complicates the interpretation of theR2from these regressions.25
20:1071:73918:9:10:75:553:97:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:This only includes transactions with a buyer that has more than 10 geographically distant (i.e., out-of-commuting zone) friends.
20:1072:74046:10:9:60:75:87:8:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f5:Having such friends is a requirement to construct the belief shifters for these individuals.26
20:1073:74140:9:10:75:553:78:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:For some of the transactions, the property is purchased by more than one individual, and we can match both individuals to their
20:1074:74267:10:9:60:553:68:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Facebook accounts. In these cases, we use the characteristics and friends’ house price experiences of the head of household, as reported
20:1075:74403:9:0:60:390:59:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:in the Acxiom data. Pooling the sets of friends of both buyers yields very similar results.

23:1178:81085:33:22:60:377:321:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:3.2  Expectation Survey: Evidence for Belief Shifters
23:1179:81138:22:15:60:553:299:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The first step of our empirical analysis is to verify that the moments of the distribution of house price experiences
23:1180:81255:15:16:60:553:284:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:across an individual’s friends affect that individual’s distribution of beliefs about future house price changes in
23:1181:81370:16:16:60:553:268:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:their own zip code. To do this, we analyze responses to a short survey conducted by Facebook in April 2017.
23:1182:81477:16:15:60:553:252:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The survey targeted Facebook users living in Los Angeles through a post on their News Feed. Figure 6 shows
23:1183:81583:15:16:60:553:237:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the survey interface. We observe 504 survey responses. The respondents’ average age was 38 years, with a 10-90
23:1184:81693:16:16:60:340:221:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:percentile range of 23 to 58 years. 62% of respondents are male.
23:1185:81757:16:15:75:553:205:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The first survey question elicits how often individuals talk to their friends about whether buying a house is a
23:1186:81868:15:16:60:553:190:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:good investment. Regular conversations with friends about housing investments are important in order for there
23:1187:81978:16:16:60:553:174:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to be a channel through which the house price experiences of friends can influence an individual’s own house price
23:1188:82092:16:15:60:553:158:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:beliefs. Panel (a) of Figure 7 shows the distribution of responses to this question. About 43% of respondents gave
23:1189:82206:15:16:60:553:143:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the modal answer, “sometimes.” The other possible responses were “never” (16% of responses), “rarely” (21% of
23:1190:82315:16:16:60:246:127:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:responses) and “often” (20% of responses).
23:1191:82357:16:15:75:553:111:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:To measure an individual’s beliefs about future house price changes, we use responses to the second question,
23:1192:82466:15:16:60:553:96:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:which asked individuals to assign probabilities to various scenarios of house price growth in their zip codes over
23:1193:82580:16:0:60:553:80:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the following 12 months. The survey enforced the assigned probabilities to add up to 100%. We use the responses
24:1194:82693:0:368:250:365:731:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Figure 6: Survey Interface
24:1195:82719:368:31:60:511:363:8:g_d0_f30:8:g_d0_f6:Note: Figure shows the interface on a user’s News Feed of the expectation survey conducted by Facebook on April 2017.
24:1196:82836:31:16:60:554:332:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to determine the mean and standard deviation of the distribution of individuals’ house price beliefs.28Panels (b)
24:1197:82949:16:16:60:553:316:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and (c) of Figure 7 show the distribution of these moments across the survey respondents. There is substantial
24:1198:83059:16:15:60:553:300:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:disagreement about expected house price growth among individuals living in the same local housing markets: while
24:1199:83171:15:16:60:553:285:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the median person expects house prices to increase by 5.3% over the next year, the 10-90 percentile range for this
24:1200:83285:16:16:60:334:269:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:estimate is 0.8% to 10.0%, and the standard deviation is 3.8%.
24:1201:83347:16:15:75:553:253:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:As a first test for whether individuals provide sensible and consistent responses to the expectation survey, Panel
24:1202:83461:15:16:60:553:238:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(d) of Figure 7 shows the distribution of the means of the belief distributions for individuals separately by their
24:1203:83576:16:16:60:553:222:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:responses to Question 3, which asks if the respondents expect the average home price in their zip code to increase,
24:1204:83691:16:15:60:553:206:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:stay the same, or decrease over the next 12 months. As a response to that question, 79.8% of individuals said they
24:1205:83805:15:16:60:553:191:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:expected house prices in their zip code to increase over the next 12 months, 14.6% said they would expect them
24:1206:83915:16:16:60:553:175:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to stay about the same, and 5.6% expected them to decline. As a reference point, in the 12 months running up
24:1207:84023:16:15:60:553:159:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to the survey, Los Angeles house prices increased by 7.4%. Individuals that thought it was most likely that house
24:1208:84136:15:16:60:553:144:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:prices in their zip code would increase over the next twelve months have a median belief about house price growth
24:1209:84249:16:16:60:553:128:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of 6.0%; the median expected house price growth of respondents who said house prices would stay about the same
24:1210:84359:16:15:60:553:112:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(decline) was 2.0% (-2.4%). While there are a small number of individuals who indicate they expect house price
24:1211:84469:15:12:68:553:97:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:28To do this, we take the mid-point of each bucket, as well as the probabilities that individual agents assign to that bucket. For
24:1212:84599:12:10:60:553:85:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:the open-ended buckets “Increase by more than 12%” and “Decrease by more than 8%”, we use point-estimates of 14% and -10%,
24:1213:84721:10:9:60:553:75:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:respectively, but our results are robust to using different values assigned to these buckets. The roughly 15% of respondents who only
24:1214:84854:9:0:60:372:66:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:assign probabilities to one bucket are assigned a standard deviation of beliefs of 0%.

26:1272:88897:32:15:60:553:353:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:mean of the belief distribution. Since all friends in Los Angeles experience the same recent house price changes,
26:1273:89010:15:16:60:553:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:across-individual variation in friends’ house price experiences is driven by differences in the experiences of their
26:1274:89126:16:16:60:553:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:out-of-commuting zone friends as well as differences in the share of friends that live locally. As we discuss below,
26:1275:89242:16:15:60:553:306:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:we want to isolate variation in friends’ experiences coming from the experiences of their out-of-commuting zone
26:1276:89353:15:16:60:553:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friends, since this allows us to address a number of potential alternative interpretations of our findings. Column
26:1277:89467:16:16:60:553:275:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:1 of Table 2 shows that a one-percentage-point (0.38 standard deviation) increase in the house price appreciation
26:1278:89580:16:15:60:553:259:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:across an individuals out-of-commuting zone friends,FriendHPExpOut−CZi,April2017, is associated with a 0.19 percentage
26:1279:89698:15:16:60:532:244:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f3:point (0.05 standard deviation) increase in the expected house price increase over the next twelve months.29
26:1280:89806:16:16:75:553:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In  column  2,  the  main  explanatory  variable  is  the  average  house  price  experience  of  all  friends,
26:1281:89917:16:15:60:553:212:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f2:FriendHPExpi,April2017, instrumented for by the average house price experiences of the individuals’ out-of-
26:1282:90024:15:16:60:553:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:commuting zone friends,FriendHPExpOut−CZi,April2017. This specification mirrors our baseline regressions in Sections
26:1283:90140:16:16:60:553:181:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:3.3 and 3.4 below, and will allow us to compare estimates across specifications with different outcome variables.
26:1284:90253:16:15:60:553:165:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A one-percentage-point increase in the house price experiences of all friends is associated with a 0.33 percentage
26:1285:90367:15:14:68:553:150:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:29The effect ofFriendHPExpOut−CZi,April2017on the R-squared is relatively modest: conditional on the control variables, the house price
26:1286:90502:14:10:60:553:136:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:experiences of an individual’s out-of-commuting zone friends explain an additional 1.1% - 1.6% of the observed variation in the mean
26:1287:90634:10:9:60:553:126:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of house price beliefs. Specifically, the OLS regression without controlling for out-of-commuting zone friends’ average house price
26:1288:90765:9:10:60:553:117:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:experiences, but including all the other control variables, has an R-squared of 0.362; the within-zip code R-squared is 0.074. When
26:1289:90896:10:9:60:553:107:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:we include the out-of-commuting zone friends’ house price experiences as an additional control variable, the R-squared increases to
26:1290:91027:9:10:60:553:98:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:0.373, and the within-zip code R-squared increases to 0.090. However, given the large sample sizes in the leverage choice regressions in
26:1291:91163:10:9:60:553:88:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Sections 3.3 and 3.4, the belief shifter has sufficient power to identify statistically significant effects of beliefs on leverage. The number
26:1292:91305:9:9:60:553:79:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of observations in Table 2 is lower than the total number of responses we observe. This is because 78 responses are from individuals
26:1293:91437:9:0:60:522:70:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:who are the only respondents from their own zip code. Their responses are thus fully explained by the zip code fixed effects.

27:1306:92825:16:15:60:412:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:statistically significant effect on the standard deviation of the belief distribution.
27:1307:92911:15:16:75:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Overall, these findings confirm that the mean and variance of the house price experiences across an individual’s
27:1308:93023:16:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friends can shift the corresponding moments of the distribution of these individuals’ house price beliefs. In the
27:1309:93136:16:32:60:504:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:following section we will use this insight to analyze the effect of house price beliefs on leverage choice.
27:1310:93243:32:22:60:169:462:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:3.3  Main Results
27:1311:93260:22:16:60:553:440:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In this section, we test how moments of the distribution of house price experiences across a homebuyer’s friends
27:1312:93372:16:15:60:553:424:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:affect that homebuyer’s leverage choice in the sample of transactions described in Section 3.1.  Based on the
27:1313:93481:15:16:60:554:409:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:evidence in Section 3.2 and in Bailey et al. (2017a), we argue that these house price experiences provide shifters
27:1314:93595:16:16:60:553:393:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of individuals’ distributions of beliefs about future house price changes that are orthogonal to other factors that
27:1315:93710:16:15:60:197:377:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:might influence leverage choice.
27:1316:93742:15:16:75:553:362:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In our baseline regression specification R1, the unit of observation is a housing transaction. We run regressions
27:1317:93855:16:16:60:553:346:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of the combined loan-to-value (CLTV) ratio for transactioniat timetin countycand financed by mortgage lender
27:1318:93963:16:15:60:553:330:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f2:lon moments of the distribution of the house price experiences of the friends of the homebuyer. This combined
27:1319:94072:15:16:60:554:315:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:loan-to-value ratio includes all mortgages originated to finance the home purchase.31We also control for a rich set
27:1320:94187:16:16:60:554:299:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of homebuyer characteristics,32and include county by purchase month by lender fixed effects,ψt,c,l.33This allows
27:1321:94299:16:15:60:553:283:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:us to compare the leverage choices across individuals who borrow from the same lender in the same county and
27:1322:94407:15:16:60:553:268:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:at the same point in time, and who therefore faced the same schedule of loan-to-value ratios and interest rates to
27:1323:94521:16:29:60:115:252:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choose from.
27:1324:94533:29:28:119:553:223:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f2:CLTVi,t,c,l=α+β1MeanFriendHPi,t+β2StDFriendHPi,t+β3Xi,t+ψt,c,l+-i,t,z,(R1)
27:1325:94607:28:15:60:554:195:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In order to use estimates ofβ1andβ2to differentiate between the housing-as-consumption scenario and the
27:1326:94710:15:12:68:553:180:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:30One can provide a structural interpretation of this estimate as a Kalman gain in the context of standard linear-gaussian bayesian
27:1327:94841:12:7:60:96:168:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:updating.
27:1328:94850:7:12:68:553:161:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:31For example, many homebuyers that choose to borrow more than 80% of the value of the house split their borrowing into a first
27:1329:94977:12:10:60:553:149:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:mortgage with an LTV of 80%, for which they do not have to pay private mortgage insurance (PMI), and a higher-interest piggy-back
27:1330:95106:10:9:60:75:139:8:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f5:loan for the remaining amount.32
27:1331:95138:9:10:75:553:130:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:With the exception of age, which can be observed at the point of the transaction, other homebuyer characteristics are observed
27:1332:95264:10:9:60:553:120:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:as of the points of the Acxiom snapshots. We assign the value from the most proximate snapshot. We flexibly control for homebuyer
27:1333:95393:9:10:60:553:111:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:income, age, household size, family status, occupation, and education level. We also control for the number of friends, the number of
27:1334:95526:10:9:60:75:101:8:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f5:out-of-commuting zone friends, and the number of counties in which an individual has friends.33
27:1335:95621:9:10:75:553:92:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Even though Hurst et al. (2016) have shown that most lenders do not vary their mortgage pricing geographically, this choice of
27:1336:95747:10:9:60:553:82:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:fixed effects is conservative, and ensures that we are comparing the leverage choices of individuals facing the same trade-offs between
27:1337:95882:9:9:60:553:73:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:making a smaller downpayment and paying higher interest rates. In the rare cases where the first and second mortgages are by different
27:1338:96016:9:0:60:425:64:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:lenders, we use the identity of the lender of the first mortgage to determine the lender fixed effect.
28:1339:96120:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:housing-as-investment scenario, we need to ensure that our key explanatory variables,MeanFriendHPand
28:1340:96220:15:16:60:554:714:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f2:StDFriendHP, only affect leverage choice through their effect on homebuyers’ beliefs about the distribution
28:1341:96327:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of future house price changes. A first concern is that individuals who have more local friends, and for whom higher
28:1342:96442:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:local house prices would thus lead to a higherMeanFriendHP, might also be more affected by this local house
28:1343:96549:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:price growth through channels other than the effect on their house price expectations. For example, if individuals
28:1344:96663:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:with more local friends were more likely to own local property, then they would be more likely to benefit from higher
28:1345:96780:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:capital gains, allowing them to make a larger downpayment on any subsequent property purchase. To rule out
28:1346:96886:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:confounding effects through such alternative channels, we estimate regression R1 using an instrumental variables
28:1347:96998:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(IV) strategy, where we instrument for the mean and standard deviation of all friends’ house price experiences
28:1348:97108:16:15:60:519:588:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f3:with the mean and standard deviation of the house price experiences of out-of-commuting zone friends.34
28:1349:97211:15:16:75:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Table 3 shows the results of regression R1. The baseline estimate in column 1, which does not control for
28:1350:97316:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:homebuyer demographics, suggests that individuals whose friends experienced a one-percentage-point higher house
28:1351:97427:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:price appreciation over the past 24 months increase their downpayment (and reduce their CLTV ratio) by between
28:1352:97537:15:16:60:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:8 and 9 basis points. A one within-zip-code-month standard deviation increase in friends’ house price experience
28:1353:97649:16:16:60:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:is thus associated with a 31 basis points larger downpayment. Combining this with the estimates from the survey
28:1354:97760:16:15:60:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:analyzed in Section 3.2 suggests that individuals that expect a one-percentage-point lower house price growth
28:1355:97869:15:16:60:553:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:over the coming twelve months would choose a0.0840.326= 26basis points lower downpayment. This finding suggests
28:1356:97980:16:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that individuals behave more like in the housing-as-consumption scenario rather than in the housing-as-investment
28:1357:98093:16:15:60:553:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:scenario.35Column 1 also shows that individuals whose friends had more dispersed house price experiences, and
28:1358:98202:15:16:60:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:who thus expected more variance in future house price changes, chose higher leverage. A one-percentage-point
28:1359:98310:16:16:60:553:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:increase in the across-friends standard deviation of house price experiences over the past 24 months is associated
28:1360:98424:16:15:60:553:400:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:with a 35 basis points increase in the combined loan-to-value ratio. This result is also consistent with the predictions
28:1361:98544:15:16:60:553:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:from the housing-as-consumption scenario: individuals that expect future house price growth to be more dispersed
28:1362:98656:16:16:60:553:369:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:perceive large house price drops to be more likely. This increases the benefits of not putting all their savings into
28:1363:98773:16:15:60:338:353:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the house, and therefore increases their optimal leverage choice.
28:1364:98838:15:16:75:553:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In column 2 of Table 3, we also control flexibly for a large number of borrower characteristics. The estimated
28:1365:98948:16:16:60:553:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:effects of the house price experiences of a person’s friends is remarkably stable with respect to the addition of these
28:1366:99067:16:15:60:553:306:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:observable controls. This provides additional evidence consistent with Bailey et al. (2017a) that the house price
28:1367:99180:15:16:60:553:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:experiences of a person’s friends in a given year are uncorrelated with that person’s observable characteristics,
28:1368:99293:16:16:60:553:275:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and reduces concerns that the results are driven by selection on unobservable buyer characteristics (see Altonji,
28:1369:99406:16:15:60:553:259:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Elder and Taber, 2005). In column 3, we interact the buyer demographics with year fixed effects. This allows, for
28:1370:99519:15:16:60:553:244:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:example, the effect of having a different education or a different profession on leverage choice to vary by year. This
28:1371:99637:16:16:60:553:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:specification provides a first test of whether our findings could be explained by correlated shocks to homebuyers
28:1372:99750:16:15:60:553:212:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and their friends. For example, one might have worried that less-educated Los Angeles residents have more friends
28:1373:99863:15:16:60:553:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in regions with a relatively less-educated population. In that case, a positive shock to the income prospects of
28:1374:99975:16:16:60:553:181:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:less-educated individuals in a given year might both raise house prices where less-educated buyers have friends, and
28:1375:100091:16:14:60:553:165:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:would increase the ability of these less-educated buyers to make larger downpayments. Our results are essentially
28:1376:100204:14:13:68:553:151:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:34The instrument has an F-Statistic above 1,500 across all specifications, largely driven by the fact that the set of out-of-commuting
28:1377:100338:13:9:60:553:138:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:zone friends, which is used to construct the instruments, is a subset of the set of all friends, which is used to construct the instrumented
28:1378:100478:9:7:60:254:129:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:variables,MeanFriendHPi,tandStDFriendHPi,t.
28:1379:100521:7:12:68:553:122:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:35This does not mean that more pessimistic individuals did not also reduce their housing market investments, as in the extreme
28:1380:100647:12:9:60:554:110:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:case with infinite collateral adjustment friction studied above. Indeed, Bailey et al. (2017a) document that individuals whose friends
28:1381:100781:9:10:60:553:101:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:experienced lower recent house price growth did buy smaller houses: a one-percentage-point smaller friends’ house price experience
28:1382:100911:10:9:60:553:91:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:was associated with purchasing a 0.3 percentage points smaller property. Instead, the estimate in column 1 of Table 3 suggests that as
28:1383:101045:9:10:60:553:82:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:individuals got more pessimistic, their desire to reduce their financial exposure to potential house price declines increased faster than
28:1384:101182:10:9:60:553:72:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:the reduction in that exposure that was possible to achieve by buying a smaller house. As a result, they ended up buying (slightly)
28:1385:101313:9:0:60:218:63:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:smaller houses with larger overall leverage.

29:1421:103060:10:9:60:553:187:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:effects. Column 4 interacts the measures of friends’ house price experiences with a dummy variable of whether the transaction was in a
29:1422:103194:9:10:60:553:178:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:recourse state. Column 5 interacts the measures of friends’ house price experiences with the mean of friends’ house price experiences.
29:1423:103328:10:9:60:553:168:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Column 6 interacts the measures of friends’ house price experiences with a dummy variable capturing zip code - months where the
29:1424:103455:9:10:60:553:159:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:average house price experiences of all buyers’ friends were above the sample median, loosely corresponding to a recovery period in the
29:1425:103589:10:9:60:553:149:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:housing market. Column 7 interacts the measures of friends’ house price experiences with the homeownership rate in the zip code of
29:1426:103719:9:10:60:554:140:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:the transaction. Standard errors are clustered at the zip code×transaction-month level. Significance Levels: * (p<0.10), ** (p<0.05),
29:1427:103852:10:0:60:111:130:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:*** (p<0.01).
30:1428:103867:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:unchanged from our baseline specification, suggesting that correlated shocks are not a key driver of our results.
30:1429:103980:15:16:60:301:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We provide additional robustness checks in Section 3.4.
30:1430:104035:16:16:75:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Column 4 of Table 3 tests the predictions from Section 2.2.1, which show that the downpayment protection
30:1431:104139:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:force at the heart of the housing-as-consumption scenario is particularly strong when the cost of mortgage default
30:1432:104253:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:is low. We proxy for the cost of default by whether or not the transaction occurred in a recourse state. In recourse
30:1433:104369:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:states, lenders can collect on debt not covered by the sale of a foreclosed property by going after other assets of the
30:1434:104488:16:15:60:554:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:defaulting borrowers. This substantially increases the cost of default for borrowers in recourse states.36Table 1
30:1435:104601:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:shows that about half of our transactions come from recourse states. Consistent with the predictions from Section
30:1436:104714:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:2.2.1 for the housing-as-consumption scenario, we find that the effect of friends’ average house price experiences
30:1437:104828:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:on individuals’ leverage choice is about twice as large for transactions in non-recourse states. Similarly, the effect
30:1438:104946:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of a higher standard deviation of friends’ house price experiences on mortgage leverage choice is over five times
30:1439:105059:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:as large in non-recourse states as it is in recourse states. This provides strong evidence that the downpayment
30:1440:105170:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:protection force, which is central to the housing-as-consumption scenario, is indeed stronger in legal environments
30:1441:105285:15:16:60:245:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that feature relatively low costs of default.
30:1442:105330:16:16:75:553:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Columns 5 and 6 of Table 3 test the predictions from Section 2.2.2, which highlight that in the housing-as-
30:1443:105437:16:15:60:553:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:consumption scenario, the effects of changes in house price beliefs on optimal leverage choice should be smaller if
30:1444:105552:15:16:60:553:479:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the baseline house price belief is more optimistic. When comparing the beliefs of relatively optimistic borrowers,
30:1445:105666:16:16:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:either in the time-series, or the cross-section, all borrowers expect to repay their mortgage most of the time, and
30:1446:105781:16:15:60:553:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:there is little disagreement about the probability of default that is central to leverage choices in the housing-
30:1447:105894:15:16:60:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:as-consumption scenario. Column 5 interacts our measures of friends’ house price experiences with the mean of
30:1448:106003:16:16:60:553:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friends’ house price experiences. The estimates show that changes in both the mean and the variance of friends’
30:1449:106114:16:15:60:553:400:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house price experiences have smaller effects for individuals’ already starting from an already higher mean house
30:1450:106226:15:16:60:553:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:price expectation.  This concavity in the effect of mean house price experiences is a central prediction of the
30:1451:106337:16:16:60:205:369:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:housing-as-consumption scenario.
30:1452:106369:16:15:75:553:353:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In column 6, we interact the mean and the standard deviation of friends’ house price experiences with a dummy
30:1453:106478:15:16:60:553:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:variable that captures whether or not the average house price experiences of all buyers in the purchase zip code-
30:1454:106591:16:16:60:553:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:month were above the sample median value. These transactions usually occur during the later years of our sample,
30:1455:106703:16:15:60:553:306:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:when the housing market had started to enter a recovery period, and when homebuyers were generally expecting
30:1456:106811:15:16:60:553:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:future house price increases (see Case, Shiller and Thompson, 2012, and Kuchler and Zafar, 2015, for evidence on
30:1457:106923:16:16:60:553:275:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:such extrapolative house price expectations). Consistent with the predictions from the housing-as-consumption
30:1458:107032:16:15:60:553:259:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:framework, the effects of both the mean and standard deviation of friends’ house price experiences on the leverage
30:1459:107146:15:16:60:553:244:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choice are significantly larger in absolute terms during the housing bust period than they are during the recovery
30:1460:107260:16:16:60:553:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:period.37This is consistent with the downpayment protection force being particularly strong during periods when
30:1461:107371:16:15:60:542:212:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:there is a comparatively large disagreement between optimists and pessimists about the probability of default.
30:1462:107481:15:16:75:553:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In column 7, we interact the mean and variance of the house price experiences of a homebuyers’ friends with
30:1463:107588:16:15:60:553:181:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the homeownership rate in the zip code of the transaction. The homeownership rate is one proxy for the extent of
30:1464:107700:15:12:68:553:166:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:36Ghent and Kudlyak (2011) analyze mortgage default behavior across U.S. states, and find strong evidence that the recourse/non-
30:1465:107828:12:9:60:553:154:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:recourse environment does indeed have significant effects on the default behavior of borrowers, as would be predicted if it induced
30:1466:107959:9:10:60:553:145:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:significant differences in the costs of default. Specifically, the authors find that at the average value of negative equity, the monthly
30:1467:108096:10:9:60:553:135:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:probability of default is 1.3 times higher in non-recourse states than it is in recourse states. All else equal, this means that it takes
30:1468:108233:9:10:60:553:126:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:8.6% more negative equity to make the probability of default in a recourse state the same as the probability of default in a non-recourse
30:1469:108370:10:9:60:75:116:8:g_d0_f6:6:g_d0_f5:state. See also the discussion in Kuchler and Stroebel (2009).37
30:1470:108434:9:10:75:553:107:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:A potential concern when interpreting these differential effects across time comes from the fact that we only observe the friendship
30:1471:108566:10:9:60:553:97:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:network at one point in time, July 2015.  This networks becomes a noisier measure of individuals’ contemporaneous network the
30:1472:108691:9:10:60:553:88:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:further back we go, which could induce increased attenuation bias for measuring friends’ house price experiences for buyers in earlier
30:1473:108825:10:9:60:553:78:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:transactions. However, given that the housing bust period preceded the housing recovery period, such an attenuation bias would
30:1474:108951:9:0:60:522:69:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:reduce the measured effect during the housing bust period, and therefore work against producing the effect that we uncover.
31:1475:109076:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the collateral adjustment frictions faced by pessimists who want to reduce their exposure to the housing market.
31:1476:109188:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In markets with a low homeownership rate, individuals wanting to live in a certain zip code (for example, because
31:1477:109301:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of proximity to work or good schools) are more likely to find a suitable rental property than individuals wanting
31:1478:109414:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to live in zip codes with extremely high homeownership rates. The estimates show that the effects of house price
31:1479:109526:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:beliefs on leverage choices are of the same direction across the entire support of the distribution of homeownership
31:1480:109642:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:rates. However, in regions with high homeownership rates, the effects of increased perceptions of the possibility
31:1481:109755:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of default on leverage choice are larger, because, in those regions, reducing the downpayment is a comparatively
31:1482:109867:15:16:60:382:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:easier way for households to reduce their exposure to the housing market.
31:1483:109940:16:16:75:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Taken together, the behavior of the homebuyers in our sample corresponds to the predictions from the housing-
31:1484:110049:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:as-consumption scenario, with a sizable collateral adjustment friction preventing relatively pessimistic homebuyers
31:1485:110164:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:from purchasing a substantially smaller home to live in.38These pessimistic homebuyers therefore make smaller
31:1486:110273:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:downpayments in order to reduce their housing market exposures, in particular in states where the cost of default
31:1487:110386:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:is comparatively smaller, and in particular during time periods when there are concerns about substantial price
31:1488:110497:15:33:60:186:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:drops in the housing market.
31:1489:110525:33:22:60:451:493:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:3.4  Robustness Checks and Ruling Out Alternative Explanations
31:1490:110587:22:15:60:553:471:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Our interpretation of the results in Section 3.3 is that the relationship between friends’ house price experiences and
31:1491:110705:15:16:60:553:456:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:leverage was driven by the effects of friends’ experiences on an individual’s belief about future house price growth.
31:1492:110822:16:16:60:553:440:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In this section, we rule out a number of alternative interpretations of the correlation between friends’ house price
31:1493:110938:16:15:60:553:424:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:experiences and individuals’ mortgage leverage choice. As we address these alternative interpretations, we note
31:1494:111049:15:16:60:553:409:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that most of these stories could have potentially explained the effect of friends’ average house price experiences
31:1495:111163:16:16:60:553:393:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:on leverage. However, the effect of the dispersion of friends’ house price experiences on leverage choices are much
31:1496:111278:16:15:60:553:377:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:harder to rationalize with alternative mechanisms, as are the differential effects across recourse/non-recourse states,
31:1497:111397:15:16:60:540:362:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:across periods with relatively high/low average beliefs, and across regions with different homeownership rates.
31:1498:111508:16:16:75:553:346:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A first concern we address is that the instruments in our baseline regression, the moments of the house price
31:1499:111617:16:15:60:553:330:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:experiences of out-of-commuting zone friends, might still be correlated with the individuals’ own house price
31:1500:111726:15:16:60:553:315:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:experiences, and therefore potentially with their own capital gains, in particular for individuals who have more
31:1501:111838:16:16:60:553:299:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friends in close-by commuting zones that have house price movements similar to those in their own commuting
31:1502:111945:16:15:60:553:283:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:zone. In column 1 of Table 4, we therefore use moments of the house price experiences of out-of-state friends as
31:1503:112057:15:16:60:553:268:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:instruments. Reassuringly, the magnitudes of the effects in this specification are, if anything, slightly larger than
31:1504:112174:16:16:60:187:252:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in our baseline specifications.
31:1505:112205:16:15:75:553:236:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In column 2 of Table 4, we restrict the sample to purchases by individuals who have more than 50 out-of-
31:1506:112309:15:16:60:553:221:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:commuting zone friends; in column 3, we restrict the sample to transactions by individuals with friends in at
31:1507:112418:16:16:60:553:205:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:least 35 counties. These specifications address concerns that the effects of the dispersion of friends’ experiences
31:1508:112533:16:15:60:553:189:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:on leverage could be driven by individuals without sufficiently many geographically distant friends to construct
31:1509:112645:15:16:60:553:174:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:powerful shifters of the belief distribution. Reassuringly, the magnitude of the estimates are very similar to those
31:1510:112761:16:16:60:183:158:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in the baseline specification.
31:1511:112791:16:15:75:554:142:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A further possible concern is that unobserved shocks to an individual’sabilityto make a downpayment in
31:1512:112893:15:16:60:553:127:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:a given year might be correlated with her friends’ house price experiences in that year.  Such an alternative
31:1513:113002:16:15:60:553:111:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:interpretation requires a shock to an individual’s wealth or income that contemporaneously moves house prices
31:1514:113111:15:12:68:553:96:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:38One caveat regarding the generalizability of these findings is that our sample is restricted to purchases by owner-occupiers, since
31:1515:113244:12:9:60:553:84:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:we cannot match buyers making investment purchases to their respective Facebook accounts. While we do not have the data to analyze
31:1516:113374:9:10:60:553:75:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:the leverage choices of these investment buyers, our theoretical framework suggests that those individuals should face a much smaller
31:1517:113507:10:0:60:539:65:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:collateral adjustment friction. They might therefore respond to increasing optimism by buying more houses with higher leverage.
32:1518:113636:0:29:242:371:731:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Table 4: Robustness Checks I

32:1554:114534:13:10:60:553:452:8:g_d0_f30:8:g_d0_f6:Note: Table shows results from regression R1, and provides robustness checks to the baseline results presented in Table 3. The unit
32:1555:114665:10:9:60:553:442:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of observation is a home transaction, the dependent variable is the combined loan-to-value ratio in the transaction. All specifications
32:1556:114800:9:10:60:553:433:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:include controls and fixed effects as in column 2 of Table 3. In columns 1, we instrument for moments of the house price experiences
32:1557:114932:10:9:60:553:423:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of the buyers total friends with the corresponding moments of the distribution of house price experiences of the buyers’ out-of-state
32:1558:115065:9:10:60:553:414:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:friends. In column 2, we restrict the sample to purchases by individuals with at least 50 out-of-commuting zone friends, in column 3
32:1559:115197:10:9:60:553:404:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:to purchases by individuals with friends in at least 35 unique counties. Column 4 also controls for the average income changes in the
32:1560:115330:9:9:60:553:395:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:counties where an individual has friends. In column 5, we focus on purchases by individuals working in geographically non-clustered
32:1561:115461:9:10:60:553:386:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:professions (e.g., teachers and lawyers). Column 6 also controls for the average foreclosure share in the counties where an individual
32:1562:115595:10:9:60:553:376:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:has friends. Column 7 only uses the house price experiences of out-of-commuting zone friends in recourse states to instrument for the
32:1563:115728:9:10:60:553:367:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:house price experiences of all friends. Standard errors are clustered at the zip code×transaction-month level. Significance Levels: *
32:1564:115861:10:32:60:198:357:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:(p<0.10), ** (p<0.05), *** (p<0.01).
32:1565:115897:32:15:60:553:325:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in geographically distant regions where she has friends.  As discussed above, our baseline regressions already
32:1566:116007:15:16:60:553:310:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:minimize the scope for such potentially confounding effects, by including year-specific controls for a large number
32:1567:116122:16:16:60:553:294:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of observable characteristics of homebuyers. We next address the one additional potential confounder that we were
32:1568:116235:16:15:60:553:278:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:able to identify. In particular, many people have friends that work in the same sector of the economy. If economic
32:1569:116349:15:16:60:553:263:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:activity in that sector features significant geographic clustering (e.g., tech in Silicon Valley), positive shocks to
32:1570:116466:16:16:60:553:247:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that sector in a given year might both enable an individual to make a larger downpayment and drive up aggregate
32:1571:116577:16:15:60:397:231:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house prices in those sector-exposed regions where the individual has friends.
32:1572:116655:15:16:75:553:216:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:To rule out this explanation of our findings, column 4 includes the average income change in the county where
32:1573:116764:16:16:60:553:200:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the person has friends as a control, in addition to the average house price change. We measure income annually at
32:1574:116877:16:15:60:553:184:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the county level using data from the Tax Statistics of Income (SOI). In addition, in column 5 we restrict the sample
32:1575:116993:15:16:60:553:169:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of transactions to those in which the homebuyer works in geographically non-clustered professions (e.g., lawyers
32:1576:117105:16:16:60:553:153:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and teachers). Any positive shocks to such professions, which might increase those individuals’ ability to make a
32:1577:117218:16:15:60:553:137:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:larger downpayment, should not affect house prices in regions where they have friends. This is because there are
32:1578:117330:15:16:60:553:122:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:no parts of the country where there are so many teachers or lawyers that positive shocks to those professions can
32:1579:117443:16:16:60:553:106:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:shift aggregate house prices. In both specifications the estimates are similar to our baseline estimates, confirming
32:1580:117559:16:15:60:553:90:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that common shocks to individuals and their friends that shift house prices in areas where their friends live cannot
32:1581:117675:15:16:60:149:75:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:explain our findings.
32:1582:117696:16:0:75:553:59:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Lastly, a potential alternative interpretation of our findings is that when house prices decline in counties where
33:1583:117812:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:an individual has friends, and there are subsequently more foreclosures in those counties, individuals might update
33:1584:117927:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:both their own house price beliefs as well as their expectations about the cost of default or the benefits of the
33:1585:118040:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:foreclosure process, both of which might also directly affect their leverage choice. To address such concerns, column
33:1586:118157:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:6 also includes measures of the share of all properties in friends’ counties that experienced a foreclosure over the
33:1587:118273:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:previous 24 months, based on data provided by Zillow. While a higher foreclosure rate in friends’ locations is
33:1588:118383:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:associated with larger leverage and a smaller downpayment, the inclusion of this additional control variable does
33:1589:118496:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:not significantly affect the estimated effect of friends’ house price experiences on mortgage leverage choice.39In
33:1590:118610:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:column 7, we only use the house price experiences of friends in recourse states to instrument for the house price
33:1591:118723:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:experiences of all friends. If most of the effect came through individuals learning about the cost of default when
33:1592:118837:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house prices fall where their friends live, then the estimated effect should be smaller when only using variation in
33:1593:118953:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house prices in recourse states where house price changes translate less into foreclosures. However, the estimated
33:1594:119067:16:22:60:266:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:coefficients are, in fact, almost identical in size.

33:1612:119850:0:215:184:428:509:9:g_d0_f43:9:g_d0_f43:Same EmployerSame College Family
33:1613:119882:215:9:60:553:294:8:g_d0_f30:8:g_d0_f6:Note: Table shows results from regression R1, and provides robustness checks to the baseline results presented in Table 3. The unit
33:1614:120013:9:10:60:553:285:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of observation is a home transaction, the dependent variable is the combined loan-to-value ratio in the transaction. All specifications
33:1615:120148:10:9:60:553:275:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:include controls and fixed effects as in column 2 of Table 3. In columns 1 and 2, we focus on purchases by people for whom we can
33:1616:120277:9:10:60:553:266:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:identify at least ten work friends, in columns 3 and 4 on purchases by people for whom we can identify at least ten college friends, and
33:1617:120413:10:9:60:553:256:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:in columns 5 and 6 on purchases by people for whom we can identify at least ten family members. In columns 1, 3, and 5, we repeat
33:1618:120542:9:10:60:553:247:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:our baseline specification on the restricted sample for comparability. In columns 2, 4, and 6, we use the house price experiences of an
33:1619:120677:10:9:60:553:237:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:individual’s geographically distant work friends, college friends, and family members, respectively, to instrument the corresponding
33:1620:120809:9:10:60:553:228:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:moments of the house price experiences among all friends. In column 7, we only include transactions of individuals who report a
33:1621:120936:10:9:60:553:218:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:hometown, and who purchase a property in their home state. We use these buyers’ out-of-state friends’ house price experiences to
33:1622:121064:9:10:60:553:209:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:instrument for the house price experiences of all friends. Standard errors are clustered at the zip code×transaction-month level.
33:1623:121193:10:27:60:280:199:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Significance Levels: * (p<0.10), ** (p<0.05), *** (p<0.01).
33:1624:121252:27:16:75:553:172:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:An additional challenge to our interpretation is the potential for wealth effects: if an individual has family in
33:1625:121365:16:16:60:553:156:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:those areas where she has friends, and if those family members own real estate, then house price increases in those
33:1626:121480:16:15:60:553:140:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:areas might increase her expected bequest. This wealth channel could then provide an alternative explanation
33:1627:121588:15:16:60:553:125:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:for why individuals make larger downpayments when their geographically distant friends have experienced higher
33:1628:121698:16:15:60:553:109:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house price increases. We rule out such an explanation in a number of ways. First, columns 1 to 6 of Table 5
33:1629:121806:15:12:68:553:94:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:39While the house price experiences and foreclosure rate across friends’ counties are naturally correlated, they are far from collinear.
33:1630:121942:12:9:60:553:82:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Specifically, the extent to which declining house prices translate into more foreclosures depends both on the legal environment across
33:1631:122076:9:10:60:553:73:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:states as well as on the initial level of equity that individuals have in their homes, which, in turn, depends on the entire house price
33:1632:122212:10:0:60:553:63:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:history in the county. In addition, there is a substantial time-lag between the initial house price decline and default/foreclosure activity.
34:1633:122355:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:separately explore the house price experiences across an individual’s social network of work friends, family friends,
34:1634:122472:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and college friends.  Since not all individuals report their family member, their employer, or their college on
34:1635:122583:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Facebook, these regressions have fewer observations than our baseline estimates. In columns 1, 3, and 5, we run
34:1636:122694:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:our baseline regression (corresponding to column 2 of Table 3), but only on the set of individuals for whom we can
34:1637:122808:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:identify at least ten work friends, family friends, and college friends. In columns 2, 4, and 6, we then run the same
34:1638:122925:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:regression, but only use the house price experiences among out-of-commuting zone work friends, family friends,
34:1639:123035:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and college friends, respectively, to instrument for all friends’ house price experiences.40While one might expect
34:1640:123149:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to inherit a house from a family member, this is much less likely for a work friend or college friend. Yet, our results
34:1641:123268:16:16:60:553:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:are very similar when exploiting variation in house price experiences across these three networks, suggesting that
34:1642:123382:16:15:60:553:588:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:wealth effects are not a key driver of our findings. Finally, in column 7 we restrict our sample to individuals who
34:1643:123497:15:16:60:553:573:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:report their hometown on Facebook, and who purchase a property in their home state. We use the house price
34:1644:123603:16:16:60:553:557:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:experiences of their out-of-state friends as an instrument. For those individuals, the exposure of their expected
34:1645:123716:16:15:60:553:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:bequest to out-of-state house price movements is more limited, since most of the properties they might inherit
34:1646:123826:15:16:60:553:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:are likely to be located in their home state, yet we find, if anything, a larger correlation between the house price
34:1647:123942:16:39:60:390:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:experiences of their geographically distant friends and their leverage choice.
34:1648:124020:39:25:60:519:471:14:g_d0_f7:14:g_d0_f7:4  House Price Beliefs and Leverage Choice: Ancillary Evidence
34:1649:124082:25:16:60:553:446:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The previous section documented that more optimistic individuals make larger downpayments, consistent with the
34:1650:124192:16:15:60:553:430:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:directional predictions from the housing-as-consumption scenario. In this section, we show that this relationship
34:1651:124305:15:16:60:553:415:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:between house price beliefs and mortgage leverage choice is consistent with various additional survey data. We
34:1652:124415:16:16:60:550:399:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:also provide evidence for the importance of the key mechanism underlying the housing-as-consumption scenario.
34:1653:124524:16:15:75:553:383:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:First, we consider data from the New York Fed Survey of Consumer Expectations to corroborate that, in
34:1654:124625:15:16:60:553:368:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:these data, respondents’ anticipated leverage choice is also decreasing in their expectations about future house
34:1655:124737:16:16:60:553:352:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:price growth, and increasing in their expected default probabilities. Second, we use SurveyMonkey to conduct a
34:1656:124847:16:15:60:553:336:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Downpayment Motivation Survey (DMS) that allows us to explore individuals’ leverage choices under various
34:1657:124952:15:16:60:553:321:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:scenarios for future house price growth.  In the survey, individuals select smaller downpayments when the
34:1658:125057:16:16:60:553:305:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:probability of large house price declines increases. We also explore the thought-process behind individuals’ leverage
34:1659:125174:16:15:60:553:289:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choices. In particular, the DMS elicits a “free text” response asking individuals to describe why they adjusted their
34:1660:125291:15:16:60:553:274:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:downpayment choice across different house price beliefs. Many individuals report to reduce their downpayments
34:1661:125400:16:16:60:553:258:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in response to a larger probability of house price declines for reasons that are aligned with the key mechanism
34:1662:125511:16:15:60:553:242:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in the housing-as-consumption scenario, namely that lower downpayments minimize the financial exposure to
34:1663:125616:15:16:60:553:227:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:adverse house price movements. We also show that financial advice websites and blogs regularly highlight the
34:1664:125724:16:16:60:553:211:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:tradeoffs behind the housing-as-consumption scenario when discussing how large a downpayment to make. Lastly,
34:1665:125833:16:15:60:553:195:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:we explore survey evidence on households’ perceived cost of mortgage default, to highlight that these perceived
34:1666:125944:15:16:60:553:180:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:default costs are often small enough to not make defaulting on a mortgage prohibitively expensive, consistent with
34:1667:126058:16:15:60:369:164:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:an important assumption behind the housing-as-consumption scenario.
34:1668:126125:15:12:68:553:149:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:40Bailey et al. (2017a) documented that the average house price experiences across these three separate out-of-commuting zone
34:1669:126250:12:9:60:553:137:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:networks are relatively uncorrelated. This suggests that homebuyers’ family friends do not, in general, live in the same parts of the
34:1670:126383:9:0:60:207:128:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:country as their work or college friends.
35:1671:126426:0:186:187:427:731:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Figure 8: Expectation of Applying for Additional Loan
35:1672:126530:186:0:107:282:545:0:Helvetica:0:Helvetica:<0%0%-5%5%-10%>10%

35:1674:126590:0:159:97:291:704:0:Helvetica:0:Helvetica:Probability Apply For Additional Loan (12 Months)
35:1675:126692:159:0:366:524:545:0:Helvetica:0:Helvetica:0%1%-10%>10%
35:1676:126704:0:0:364:522:694:0:Helvetica:0:Helvetica:By Expected Probability of Foreclosure (12 months)
35:1677:126754:0:190:346:540:704:0:Helvetica:0:Helvetica:Probability Apply For Additional Loan (12 Months)
35:1678:126803:190:10:60:553:514:8:g_d0_f30:8:g_d0_f6:Note: Figure shows summary statistics based on data from the FRBNY Survey of Consumer Expectations. The left panel shows the
35:1679:126927:10:9:60:553:504:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:average probability that survey respondents assign to applying for an additional loan on their property over the next 12 months, in
35:1680:127058:9:10:60:553:495:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:percent, separately by groups of expected house price growth over the next 12 months. The right panel shows the average probability
35:1681:127189:10:9:60:553:485:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of applying for an additional loan separately by the probability that individuals assign to having their house foreclosed over the next
35:1682:127324:9:30:60:101:476:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:12 months.
35:1683:127334:30:22:60:345:446:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:4.1  NY Fed Survey of Consumer Expectations.
35:1684:127378:22:16:60:553:424:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We first analyze survey data from the 2014 Real Estate and Housing Module of the New York Fed Survey of
35:1685:127481:16:16:60:553:408:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Consumer Expectations (SCE).41Within these data, we consider the determinants of individuals’ (anticipated)
35:1686:127588:16:15:60:553:392:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:leverage choice. Our key outcome variable is the answer to the following question asked of current homeowners:
35:1687:127698:15:16:60:553:377:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:“What is the percent chance that over the next 12 months you will apply for an additional loan on your primary
35:1688:127808:16:16:60:553:361:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:residence?” In Figure 8, we explore how the answer to this question differs with respondents’ expectations. In the
35:1689:127922:16:15:60:553:345:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:left panel, we sort respondents by their beliefs about house price growth in their neighborhood over the coming
35:1690:128033:15:16:60:553:330:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:12 months, which was also elicited in the survey. In the right panel, we sort respondents by their answer to the
35:1691:128145:16:16:60:553:314:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:following question: “What is the percent chance that over the next 12 months you will enter foreclosure or lose
35:1692:128256:16:15:60:217:298:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:your home through a repossession?”
35:1693:128290:15:16:75:553:283:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In both panels, there is evidence that individuals who are more pessimistic are more likely to want to increase
35:1694:128401:16:15:60:553:267:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:leverage going forward, thereby reducing their financial exposure to the housing market. However, while these
35:1695:128510:15:16:60:553:252:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:results are highly consistent with the key mechanism in the housing-as-consumption scenario, there are other
35:1696:128618:16:16:60:553:236:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:possible explanations for the observed correlations. For example, in regions where the economy is doing badly,
35:1697:128728:16:15:60:553:220:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:individuals might expect house prices to decline and might also fear they have to tap into home equity to smooth
35:1698:128840:15:16:60:553:205:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:out income shocks. Such possibly confounding stories highlight the value of the quasi-orthogonal belief shifters in
35:1699:128955:16:15:60:492:189:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:our main empirical analysis for identifying causal relationships between beliefs and leverage choice.
35:1700:129056:15:12:68:553:174:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:41The Survey of Consumer Expectations is a monthly online survey of a rotating panel of household heads launched by the Federal
35:1701:129183:12:10:60:553:162:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Reserve Bank of New York in 2013. A household head is defined as the person in the household who owns, is buying, or rents the home.
35:1702:129315:10:9:60:553:152:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:It is nationally representative. New respondents are drawn monthly to match demographic targets from the American Community
35:1703:129438:9:10:60:553:143:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Survey (ACS), and stay on the panel for up to twelve months before rotating out. The Housing Module is an annual 30-minute add-on
35:1704:129567:10:9:60:553:133:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:to the SCE conducted in February; the set of questions varies by year. In the 2014 implementation, 85% of SCE household heads
35:1705:129692:9:0:60:243:124:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:completed the module, for a sample size of 1,213.
36:1706:129743:0:22:60:289:729:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:4.2  Downpayment Motivation Survey
36:1707:129777:22:16:60:553:707:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:To provide direct evidence on the motivations behind individuals’ mortgage choices, we designed a short
36:1708:129880:16:15:60:553:691:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Downpayment Motivation Survey (DMS) to elicit how and why individuals’ mortgage choices depend on their
36:1709:129983:15:16:60:553:676:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:beliefs about future house price changes.  The most important purpose of this survey is to provide direct
36:1710:130088:16:16:60:553:660:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:evidence that the mechanism behind the housing-as-consumption scenario features regularly in the narratives
36:1711:130195:16:15:60:336:644:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that individuals have about their own mortgage choice process.
36:1712:130257:15:16:75:553:629:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The DMS was administered to current homeowners via SurveyMonkey in September 2017. The demographics
36:1713:130356:16:15:60:553:613:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of the survey respondents are described in Appendix C.3. The survey presented individuals with different scenarios
36:1714:130470:15:16:60:553:598:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:about future house price growth, and asked them to recommend one of three possible downpayment choices to a
36:1715:130577:16:24:60:530:582:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f3:friend. Specifically, the first wave of the survey, administered to 826 respondents, was designed as follows:42
36:1716:130688:24:15:85:528:558:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Your friend is buying a house, and is asking you for advice on what downpayment to choose. The
36:1717:130782:15:20:85:394:543:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house costs $100,000, and your friend has a total of $30,000 in savings.
36:1718:130854:20:16:85:528:523:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f2:[OPTIMISTIC SCENARIO.]Your friend believes that over the next few years there is an equal
36:1719:130943:16:15:85:528:507:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:50% chance that house prices will eitherstay the sameorincreaseby 25%. If the bank offers him the
36:1720:131040:15:20:85:475:492:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:following three 30-year mortgages, which mortgage would you advise your friend to take?
36:1721:131127:20:20:85:296:472:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(i) Downpayment of $20,000, interest rate of 3%
36:1722:131174:20:19:85:298:452:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(ii) Downpayment of $10,000, interest rate of 4%
36:1723:131222:19:32:85:296:433:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(iii) Downpayment of $3,000, interest rate of 5%
36:1724:131270:32:16:85:529:401:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f2:[PESSIMISTIC SCENARIO.]If your friend instead believes that over the next few years there
36:1725:131359:16:15:85:529:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:is an equal 50% chance that house prices will eitherstay the sameordecreaseby 25%, which of the
36:1726:131454:15:20:85:343:370:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:following 30-year mortgages would you advise him to take?
36:1727:131511:20:20:85:296:350:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(i) Downpayment of $20,000, interest rate of 3%
