36:1729:131606:19:24:85:296:311:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(iii) Downpayment of $3,000, interest rate of 5%
36:1730:131654:24:16:60:553:287:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The order of the mortgage options and the order of the optimistic and pessimistic scenarios were randomized
36:1731:131761:16:15:60:553:271:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:across respondents. If the respondent did not change the downpayment recommendation across the two house
36:1732:131865:15:16:60:553:256:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:price scenarios, we asked the following free-text question: “Why did you decide not to change the mortgage
36:1733:131971:16:15:60:553:240:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:recommendation for your friend?”; if the respondent did change the downpayment recommendation, we asked:
36:1734:132075:15:16:60:366:225:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:“Why did you change the mortgage recommendation for your friend?”
36:1735:132140:16:16:75:553:209:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The second wave of the survey, which had 794 respondents, presented different house price scenarios.  In
36:1736:132244:16:15:60:553:193:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that wave, the optimistic scenario was house prices that stayed flat with certainty, while the pessimistic scenario
36:1737:132359:15:16:60:553:178:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:involved an equal probability each of a 25% increase and a 25% decrease in house prices. We call this second
36:1738:132467:16:16:60:553:162:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:scenario, which involves a mean-preserving increase in the spread, more pessimistic, because it entails a higher
36:1739:132579:16:15:60:262:146:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:probability of substantial house price declines.
36:1740:132627:15:16:75:553:131:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:For each of the two survey waves, we begin by exploring if and how individuals changed their leverage choices
36:1741:132736:16:16:60:553:115:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:across the two house price scenarios. The left column of Figure 9 analyzes responses to the first survey wave,

38:1840:137664:218:10:60:553:323:8:g_d0_f30:8:g_d0_f6:Note: Table shows comments from the free-text question in the Downpayment Motivation Survey among those individuals who

38:1848:138686:10:9:60:553:247:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:scenario. The bottom row shows responses from individuals who were first shown the pessimistic scenario, and then recommended a
38:1849:138813:9:15:60:258:238:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:larger downpayment for the more optimistic scenario.
38:1850:138865:15:12:68:553:223:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:43Across all house price scenarios and survey waves, the most common recommendation is to make a downpayment of 20%. In
38:1851:138984:12:9:60:553:211:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:addition, the majority of respondents did not change their downpayment recommendation in response to changes in house price
38:1852:139107:9:10:60:553:202:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:expectations.  This is consistent with other factors besides beliefs playing an important role in determining individuals’ leverage
38:1853:139238:10:9:60:553:192:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:choices. The “free text” responses of those individuals who did not change their recommendation provide some insights into what
38:1854:139365:9:10:60:553:183:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:these reasons are. Very common explanations for keeping the downpayment recommendation fixed, usually at 20%, are a desire to
38:1855:139490:10:9:60:553:173:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:reduce the interest rate as much as possible (e.g., “Choice is based on objective to reduce interest as much as possible”; “No matter
38:1856:139623:9:10:60:553:164:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:what, better to have lower interest rate on smaller mortgage”) or to borrow as little as possible (e.g., “Debt, even mortgage debt,
38:1857:139754:10:9:60:553:154:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:makes me uncomfortable.”; “Proverbs 22:7, Romans 13:8”; “Debt is a four-letter word. Don’t borrow one penny more than you need
38:1858:139880:9:10:60:553:145:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:to. Most people live in their homes for more than a few years, anyway.”). Consistent with this last response, a second set of responses
38:1859:140015:10:9:60:553:135:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:suggested that house price beliefs didn’t matter so much for households that did not plan on selling/moving any time soon (e.g.,
38:1860:140143:9:10:60:553:126:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:“Because the interest rate makes the biggest difference to savings over time. Nothing indicated the friend might want to sell in the
38:1861:140275:10:9:60:553:116:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:near future.”; “Because it did not state whether or not he would be staying in the house long term.”) Finally, a number of individuals
38:1862:140409:9:9:60:553:107:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:explicitly recommend at least a 20% downpayment, if at all possible, in order to avoid having to pay private mortgage insurance (“By
38:1863:140541:9:10:60:553:98:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:putting down 20% they eliminate the required mortgage insurance”; “with 20% down you do not have to have mortgage insurance,
38:1864:140665:10:7:60:158:88:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:which is a great savings”).
38:1865:140692:7:12:68:553:81:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:44Individuals are substantially more likely to report a lower downpayment recommendation in the pessimistic scenario if they first
38:1866:140822:12:0:60:485:69:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:get presented with the more optimistic scenario than if they first get presented with the more pessimistic scenario.
39:1867:140940:0:15:75:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:While it is reassuring to find the same relationship between house price beliefs and mortgage leverage choice
39:1868:141049:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in the Downpayment Motivation Survey as in the detailed empirical analysis in Section 3.3, the primary benefit
39:1869:141159:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of the DMS is our ability to investigate the mechanism behind this relationship, by directly asking individuals to
39:1870:141273:16:15:60:553:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:explain why they suggested a smaller downpayment in the more pessimistic house price scenario. Table 6 presents
39:1871:141384:15:16:60:553:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:a number of representative explanations by the survey respondents. Importantly, many of the explanations reflect
39:1872:141496:16:16:60:553:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:a desire to reduce the financial exposure to the housing asset in case of higher expected default probabilities, which
39:1873:141614:16:15:60:553:635:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:maps directly into the mechanism behind the housing-as-consumption scenario.45Indeed, a number of individuals
39:1874:141723:15:16:60:553:620:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:explicitly mention that through bankruptcy or default a smaller downpayment can allow individuals to avoid the
39:1875:141833:16:32:60:309:604:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:potential capital loss coming from declining house prices.
39:1876:141891:32:22:60:246:572:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:4.3  Financial Advice Websites
39:1877:141921:22:16:60:553:550:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Many homebuyers turn to the internet for help in the home purchase and mortgage choice process (see Piazzesi,
39:1878:142030:16:16:60:553:534:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Schneider and Stroebel, 2015). Indeed, financial advice websites and blogs regularly discuss the tradeoffs behind
39:1879:142143:16:15:60:553:518:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:choosing the size of the downpayment, and therefore offer another window into how considerations about future
39:1880:142252:15:16:60:553:503:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:house price changes affect leverage choices. In surveying these financial advice websites, we came across many
39:1881:142362:16:16:60:553:487:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:instances of financial advisors highlighting the risk-shifting benefits of taking out smaller mortgages that are at the
39:1882:142481:16:15:60:553:471:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:heart of the mechanism in the housing-as-consumption scenario. For example, Ric Edelman (2014), the number-
39:1883:142588:15:16:60:553:456:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:one independent financial adviser as ranked by Barron’s in 2009, 2010, and 2012, described the benefits of a large
39:1884:142702:16:24:60:148:440:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:mortgage as follows:
39:1885:142722:24:15:85:528:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Have you noticed that your home is worth much more than it was 10 years ago? You might be worried
39:1886:142819:15:16:85:528:401:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that your home’s value will fall. If you’re afraid that your home’s value might decline, you should sell
39:1887:142923:16:16:85:528:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the house before that happens. But you don’t want to do that! It’s your home, after all. You have
39:1888:143020:16:15:85:528:369:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:roots in the community. Uproot the kids? And where would you move? No, selling is not a practical
39:1889:143117:15:16:85:528:354:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:idea. Still, you fret that your home’s equity is at risk. Can you protect it without having to sell? Yes!
39:1890:143222:16:16:85:528:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Simply get a new mortgage, and pull the equity out of the house. It’s the same thing as selling, except
39:1891:143325:16:23:85:203:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that you don’t have to sell!
39:1892:143353:23:16:60:553:299:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In the language of our model: pessimistic homebuyers with a large collateral adjustment friction can reduce their
39:1893:143466:16:16:60:553:283:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:exposure to house price changes by taking on higher leverage. Similarly, the following discussion on The Mortgage
39:1894:143579:16:15:60:553:267:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Reports (2017) highlights that making a 20% downpayment may not be the ideal choice, since “you’re at risk when
39:1895:143690:15:24:60:426:252:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:[your] home value drops. A down payment protects the bank, not the home buyer:”
39:1896:143769:24:16:85:528:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Home values are tied to the U.S. economy. Most of the time, the economy is making incremental gains,
39:1897:143869:16:15:85:528:212:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and home prices rise. But sometimes, the economy falters. This usually happens after extended periods
39:1898:143970:15:20:85:510:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of too-hot growth. That happened in the late 2000s. In this situation, consider two home buyers:
39:1899:144066:20:19:85:292:177:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:- Buyer A: Puts 20% down on a $300,000 home
39:1900:144109:19:15:85:349:158:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:- Buyer B: Uses FHA to put 3.5% down on a $300,000 home
39:1901:144164:15:12:68:553:143:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:45While respondents usually provided a reasonable explanation for why they did or did not change their mortgage recommendation,
39:1902:144291:12:10:60:553:131:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:there is a fair amount of noise in the responses (e.g., respondents posting random YouTube links). In addition, some individuals
39:1903:144419:10:9:60:553:121:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:responded with comments along the lines of “no clue,” “not sure,” and “just a feeling.” The presence of such responses is unsurprising
39:1904:144553:9:10:60:553:112:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:given the non-incentivized nature of the survey. Also, when asked why they changed their mortgage recommendation, a small number
39:1905:144681:10:9:60:553:102:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:of individuals responded “I did not.” Investigating these cases suggests that they were most likely responses from individuals who
39:1906:144811:9:10:60:553:93:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:had picked the answer in the same “position” as their previous one, but which, due to the randomization of the downpayment choices
39:1907:144941:10:9:60:553:83:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:presented to respondents, now corresponded to a different downpayment recommendation. The complete set of survey responses is
39:1908:145066:9:0:60:201:74:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:available from the authors on request.
40:1909:145106:0:15:85:528:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Buyer A, thinking he is being “conservative” puts $60,000 down on a home. Buyer B puts down just
40:1910:145202:15:16:85:528:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:$10,500. If home values fall 20% neither Buyer A nor Buyer B have any equity in their homes. However,
40:1911:145303:16:16:85:528:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Buyer A lost a much bigger amount. Plus, Buyer B carries less risk of being foreclosed on if she can
40:1912:145403:16:15:85:528:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:no longer make her payments. This is because banks know they will take a bigger loss repossessing a
40:1913:145502:15:16:85:528:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:home with a larger outstanding loan balance. So, really, which home buyer is more conservative? The
40:1914:145601:16:24:85:249:651:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:one who puts the least amount down.
40:1915:145636:24:15:60:553:627:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Appendix C.1 shows additional examples of financial advice websites and blogs highlighting that one benefit of small
40:1916:145752:15:16:60:553:612:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:downpayments is the limited exposure to potential house price declines. This evidence, combined with the responses
40:1917:145866:16:16:60:553:596:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to the Downpayment Motivation Survey analyzed in Section 4.2, provides evidence for the channel through which
40:1918:145975:16:15:60:553:580:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:concerns about falling house prices can motivate individuals to make smaller downpayments. Importantly, we
40:1919:146081:15:16:60:553:565:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:do not want to suggest that house beliefs are the only, or even the most important determinant of mortgage
40:1920:146187:16:16:60:553:549:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:leverage choice. However, to the extent that beliefs do affect households’ downpayment decision, there appears to
40:1921:146300:16:15:60:553:533:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:be strong support for the mechanism through which more pessimistic individuals choose lower downpayments in
40:1922:146407:15:33:60:222:518:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:the housing-as-consumption scenario.
40:1923:146443:33:22:60:252:485:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:4.4  Beliefs about Default Costs
40:1924:146475:22:15:60:553:463:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:One important reason why more pessimistic individuals make smaller downpayments in the housing-as-
40:1925:146573:15:16:60:553:448:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:consumption scenario is their perception that walking away from an underwater mortgage is not overly costly,
40:1926:146681:16:16:60:553:432:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:either financially or socially. This is very consistent with some of the responses from the Downpayment Motivation
40:1927:146795:16:15:60:553:416:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Survey presented in Table 6. In this final section, we explore additional data to investigate households’ beliefs
40:1928:146908:15:16:60:214:401:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:about the cost of mortgage default.
40:1929:146943:16:16:75:553:385:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We first analyze responses to the following question from the SCE described in Section 4.1: “If somebody with
40:1930:147052:16:15:60:553:369:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:a mortgage like yours and living in your state went through foreclosure, do you think their lender could legally
40:1931:147164:15:16:60:553:354:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:go after some of their other assets (e.g. bank accounts, cars, other property, etc.) to cover the remaining amount
40:1932:147278:16:16:60:553:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:they owe?” 55% or respondents said that the bank could legally go after other assets, 45% responded that the
40:1933:147386:16:15:60:553:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:bank could not. (As discussed above, the true legal authority of the bank depends on state-level recourse laws.
40:1934:147497:15:16:60:553:307:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Unfortunately, the public release data from the SCE do not contain state identifiers that would allow us to explore
40:1935:147612:16:16:60:553:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:whether individual perceptions of banks’ legal authority are correlated with banks’ true legal authority). However,
40:1936:147727:16:15:60:553:275:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:only 77% of those respondents who said that the bank could go after their other assets (which corresponds to 42%
40:1937:147839:15:16:60:553:260:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of all respondents) believed that the bankwouldactually do this.46This suggests that the majority of survey
40:1938:147946:16:16:60:553:244:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:respondents believe that walking away from an underwater mortgage would allow them to discard any negative
40:1939:148052:16:15:60:248:228:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:equity without putting other assets at risk.
40:1940:148096:15:16:75:553:213:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We also review the evidence reported in Guiso, Sapienza and Zingales (2013), who conducted a quarterly survey
40:1941:148205:16:16:60:553:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:on the mortgage default beliefs and attitudes of a representative sample of U.S. households between December 2008
40:1942:148318:16:15:60:553:181:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:and September 2010. Consistent with the evidence from the SCE, respondents reported an average probability
40:1943:148424:15:16:60:553:166:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:of 53.4% that lenders would go after other assets of defaulters.47In addition, 23% of respondents said that they
40:1944:148536:16:16:60:553:150:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:would walk away from their house (default on their mortgage) if they had more than $100,000 negative equity
40:1945:148643:16:14:60:553:134:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:in the house, even if they were able to repay their loans; this is despite the fact that 82% of respondents argued
40:1946:148757:14:13:68:553:120:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:46The precise question from the SCE is: “If somebody with a mortgage like yours and living in your state went through foreclosure,
40:1947:148887:13:9:60:553:107:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:do you think their lender actually would go after some of their other assets (e.g. bank accounts, cars, other property, etc.) to cover
40:1948:149021:9:7:60:186:98:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:the remaining amount they owe?”
40:1949:149052:7:12:68:553:91:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:47The exact question was: “When people default on their mortgage, the lender repossesses the house. Sometimes the mortgage is
40:1950:149177:12:9:60:553:79:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:more than the value of the house. On a scale from 0 to 100, where 0 equals “absolutely no chance” and 100 equals “absolutely certain”
40:1951:149310:9:10:60:553:70:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:what do you expect are the chances that the lenders will go after people who default on their mortgage for the full amount of the
40:1952:149439:10:0:60:102:60:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:mortgage?”
41:1953:149451:0:15:60:553:729:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that it was morally wrong to default on a mortgage if it were possible to keep making mortgage payments. While
41:1954:149561:15:16:60:553:714:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:this survey suggests that default costs appear low enough that a substantial proportion of the population would
41:1955:149672:16:16:60:553:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:consider defaulting strategically, recall that strategic default is not a necessary condition for our mechanism to
41:1956:149786:16:32:60:159:682:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:work (see Section 1.2).
41:1957:149809:32:22:60:272:650:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:4.5  Collateral Adjustment Friction
41:1958:149844:22:16:60:553:628:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:The behavior of households documented in Section 3 implies the presence of a significant collateral adjustment
41:1959:149954:16:15:60:553:612:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friction. As discussed above, this friction captures that individuals’ decisions about what house to buy are not
41:1960:150066:15:16:60:553:597:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:just determined by their investment motives, but also by their consumption motives. A high collateral adjustment
41:1961:150178:16:16:60:553:581:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:friction means that more pessimistic homebuyers could not reduce their exposure to house price movements by
41:1962:150285:16:15:60:553:565:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:buying a smaller property or moving to a different neighborhood without a substantial decline in their utility from
41:1963:150400:15:16:60:553:550:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:living in their house. Instead, these pessimistic households chose to reduce their downpayment to minimize their
41:1964:150512:16:16:60:553:534:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:housing market exposure. In this section, we analyze data from Zillow’s “Consumer Housing Trends Report 2017”
41:1965:150621:16:15:60:430:518:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to provide direct evidence for the presence of a sizable collateral adjustment friction.
41:1966:150709:15:16:75:553:503:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:In the survey, a sample of about 3,000 representative homebuyers were asked about the requirements in the
41:1967:150814:16:16:60:553:487:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:selection of their property. With respect to location, 71% of buyers required the home to be in a safe neighborhood,
41:1968:150930:16:15:60:553:471:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:40% required off-street parking, 39% required that the house was in their preferred neighborhood, and 29% required
41:1969:151044:15:16:60:553:456:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:that the house was in their preferred school district. Other neighborhood requirements were proximity to shopping
41:1970:151157:16:16:60:553:440:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(29% of respondents), proximity to work (28%), proximity to family and friends (26%) and proximity to public
41:1971:151265:16:15:60:553:424:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:transportation (18%). With respect to the actual property, 67% of respondents required the home to be within
41:1972:151373:15:16:60:553:409:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:their initial price range, 62% required that the property had air conditioning, 62% required that it had their
41:1973:151483:16:16:60:553:393:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:preferred number of bedrooms, and 53% that it had the preferred number of bathrooms.  Other requirements
41:1974:151587:16:15:60:553:377:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:were that the property had private outdoor space (48% of respondents), that it had the preferred square footage
41:1975:151698:15:16:60:553:362:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:(47%), that it had a layout that fit the buyers’ preferences (47%), that it had ample storage (41%), and that it
41:1976:151810:16:16:60:553:346:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:had the buyers’ preferred utilities (40%). Only 40% of respondents required that the property had good potential
41:1977:151922:16:15:60:553:330:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to increase in value.  This suggests that, consistent with a sizable collateral adjustment frictions, homebuyers
41:1978:152034:15:39:60:535:315:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:regularly perceive the consumption aspect of the property to be more important than the investment aspect.
41:1979:152140:39:25:60:161:276:14:g_d0_f7:14:g_d0_f7:5  Conclusion
41:1980:152153:25:16:60:553:251:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:We develop a parsimonious model of mortgage leverage determination in the presence of heterogeneous beliefs
41:1981:152260:16:16:60:553:235:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to show that the relationship between homebuyers’ optimism and leverage crucially depends on the degree of
41:1982:152366:16:15:60:553:219:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:collateral adjustment frictions faced by homebuyers. When households primarily maximize the levered return of
41:1983:152475:15:16:60:553:204:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:their property investment (small collateral adjustment friction), more pessimistic homebuyers reduce their leverage
41:1984:152590:16:16:60:553:188:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:to purchase smaller houses (housing-as-investment scenario). However, when the collateral adjustment friction is
41:1985:152702:16:15:60:553:172:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:large and considerations such as family size pin down the desired property size, the only way for pessimistic
41:1986:152811:15:16:60:553:157:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:homebuyers to reduce the size of their exposure to the housing market is by reducing their downpayment and
41:1987:152917:16:16:60:553:141:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:increasing their leverage (housing-as-consumption scenario). Our empirical findings show that borrowers’ beliefs
41:1988:153029:16:15:60:553:125:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:indeed play an important role in determining borrowers’ leverage decisions.  We show that more pessimistic
41:1989:153135:15:16:60:553:110:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:individuals take on more leverage, in particular during periods of declining house prices, and in particular in states
41:1990:153253:16:16:60:553:94:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:with a relatively low cost of default. These findings are consistent with a large collateral adjustment friction, and
41:1991:153370:16:0:60:313:78:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:map most closely to the housing-as-consumption scenario.

49:2630:172524:13:12:60:553:394:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:offer more attractive LTV schedules at the margin, so borrowers substitute towards higher leverage. More optimistic lenders
49:2631:172647:12:13:60:553:382:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:offer more attractive schedule, which makes borrowers effectively richer at date0, reducing their need to borrow, which
49:2632:172766:13:29:60:540:369:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:reduces their equilibrium leverage. The results in both scenarios, when combined, establish the result in Proposition 4.

51:2882:178689:34:25:60:286:204:14:g_d0_f7:14:g_d0_f7:B  Non-Parametric Predictions
51:2883:178718:25:19:60:203:179:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:B.1  Theoretical results
51:2884:178742:19:12:60:553:160:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:While the parametric assumption underlying Propositions 1 through 3 are natural, given that the distribution ofgis
51:2885:178856:12:13:60:553:148:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:uni-modal and symmetric in the data, these results impose a priori unnecessary distributional restrictions. An alternative
51:2886:178978:13:12:60:553:135:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:approach is to consider cross-sectional comparisons among borrowers that do not rely on parametric assumptions. This
51:2887:179094:12:13:60:553:123:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:approach uses the ability to potentially observe shifts of the whole distribution of beliefs, which is a unique feature of our
51:2888:179220:13:13:60:124:110:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:empirical setup.
51:2889:179236:13:12:75:553:97:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:In general, there are numerous ways in which one borrower may be “more optimistic” than another in a non-parametric
51:2890:179351:12:14:60:553:85:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:sense.  For example, two borrowers could disagree primarily about the probability of very large declines or very large
51:2891:179469:14:12:68:553:71:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:50By slightly changing the default formulation, one can allow for borrowers to save at date0to use some of those funds to avoid
51:2892:179596:12:0:60:554:59:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:default at date1. In that case, Equations A2 and A3 still determine the equilibrium after accounting for the different default behavior.
52:2893:179734:0:12:60:553:729:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:increases in house prices. In this section, we identify the appropriate definition of stochastic dominance that allows us to
52:2894:179858:12:13:60:553:717:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:provide unambiguous directional predictions for borrowers’ leverage choices across both the housing-as-consumption and
52:2895:179976:13:13:60:553:704:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:housing-as-investment scenarios. Because of the inherent difficulties with establishing general comparisons between infinite-
52:2896:180101:13:12:60:553:691:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:dimensional distributions, our non-parametric results only provide a partial order when comparing borrowers in the data
52:2897:180220:12:13:60:553:679:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:— there are many pairwise comparisons of borrowers’ distributions that cannot be ranked according to the appropriate
52:2898:180336:13:12:60:134:666:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:dominance notion.
52:2899:180353:12:13:75:553:654:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:We use three different stochastic orders to define optimism.  Given two distributions with cumulative distribution
52:2900:180467:13:0:60:217:641:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:functionsFj(·)andFi(·)with support
52:2901:180501:0:8:220:224:649:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:[
52:2902:180502:8:0:224:237:641:9:g_d0_f49:9:g_d0_f49:g,g
52:2903:180505:0:8:237:241:649:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:]
52:2904:180506:8:13:243:554:641:9:g_d0_f49:9:g_d0_f48:,we define truncated expectation stochastic dominance, first-order stochastic
52:2905:180583:13:16:60:260:628:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:dominance and hazard rate dominance as follows:

52:2960:181971:0:9:321:336:274:4:g_d0_f59:6:g_d0_f63:fi( ̃δ)
52:2961:181978:9:17:316:353:265:4:g_d0_f65:6:g_d0_f61:1−Fi( ̃δ),∀ ̃δ
52:2962:181992:17:34:254:378:248:6:g_d0_f63:6:g_d0_f63:Hazard Rate Stochastic Dominance
52:2963:182024:34:13:60:553:214:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:We have showed that in the housing-as-investment scenario, whenκi= 0, there are clear predictions for the leverage choices
52:2964:182146:13:12:60:553:201:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:across two borrowers whose belief distributions could be ranked according to truncated-expectation stochastic dominance.
52:2965:182266:12:13:60:553:189:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:We also showed that in the housing-as-consumption scenario, clear predictions were obtained for belief distributions that
52:2966:182387:13:12:60:553:176:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:could be ranked according to first-order stochastic dominance.  However, neither of these dominance concepts implies
52:2967:182503:12:13:60:553:164:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:the other, so it is unclear how to compare belief distributions if we are ex-ante agnostic about whether the housing-as-
52:2968:182623:13:13:60:554:151:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:consumption or the housing-as-investment scenario applies, especially whenκi>0.  Therefore, we adopt hazard rate
52:2969:182735:13:12:60:553:138:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:dominance as the appropriate definition of optimism that allows to test non-parametrically for the effect of beliefs on
52:2970:182854:12:21:60:372:126:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:leverage regardless of the underlying scenario, as formalized in Proposition 5.
52:2971:182933:21:12:60:554:105:9:g_d0_f53:9:g_d0_f50:Proposition 5.(Non-parametric predictions.)Compare two borrowersiandjwith different distributionsFi(·)andFj(·)
52:2972:183043:12:13:60:245:93:9:g_d0_f54:9:g_d0_f54:about the growth rate of house prices changes.
52:2973:183089:13:13:60:554:80:9:g_d0_f54:9:g_d0_f54:a) In the housing-as-investment scenario, ifFj(·)dominatesFi(·)in a hazard rate sense (or in a truncated expectation
52:2974:183205:13:0:60:502:67:9:g_d0_f54:9:g_d0_f54:sense whenκi= 0), all else equal, borrowerjchooses a higher LTV ratio and a larger house than borroweri.
53:2975:183311:0:12:60:554:729:9:g_d0_f54:9:g_d0_f54:b) In the housing-as-consumption scenario, ifFj(·)dominatesFi(·)in a hazard rate sense (or in a first-order sense), all
53:2976:183430:12:21:60:420:717:9:g_d0_f54:9:g_d0_f54:else equal, borrowerjchooses a lower LTV ratio and lower consumption than borroweri.
53:2977:183514:21:13:60:553:696:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Proposition 5 shows that borrowers’ optimism, measured as hazard rate dominance, has opposite predictions in the polar
53:2978:183632:13:12:60:553:683:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:scenarios we consider for any value ofκi. More optimistic borrowers take on more leverage in the housing-as-investment
53:2979:183750:12:13:60:553:671:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:scenario, but they take on less leverage in the housing-as-consumption scenario. These results generalize the insights from
53:2980:183873:13:12:60:553:658:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:the case with normally distributed beliefs, but they are not exactly identical. We show in the Appendix that, when beliefs
53:2981:183995:12:13:60:553:646:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:are normally distributed, a distribution with a higher mean dominates in a hazard rate sense a distribution with a lower
53:2982:184115:13:13:60:553:633:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:mean (holding its variance constant).  Hence, when comparing means in the normal case, the results of Proposition 1
53:2983:184230:13:12:60:553:620:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:are implied by those of Proposition 5. However, we also show that, in the normal context, a distribution with a higher
53:2984:184348:12:13:60:553:608:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:variance does not dominate in a hazard rate sense a distribution with a lower variance (holding its mean constant). This
53:2985:184468:13:12:60:553:595:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:illustrates that hazard rate dominance is only a sufficient (not a necessary) condition to derive unambiguous predictions for
53:2986:184593:12:27:60:274:583:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:the relationships between beliefs and leverage choice.
53:2987:184647:27:18:60:328:556:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:Proof of Proposition 5. (Non-parametric predictions)

53:3131:185909:0:23:402:407:276:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
53:3132:185910:23:0:376:400:253:9:g_d0_f50:6:g_d0_f21:1−Fi
53:3133:185914:0:8:402:412:261:10:g_d0_f18:9:g_d0_f50:( ̃
53:3134:185917:8:0:407:411:253:9:g_d0_f49:9:g_d0_f49:δ
53:3135:185918:0:0:411:422:261:10:g_d0_f18:9:g_d0_f49:)h
53:3136:185920:0:7:424:434:268:10:g_d0_f18:9:g_d0_f50:( ̃
53:3137:185923:7:0:428:432:261:9:g_d0_f49:9:g_d0_f49:δ
53:3138:185924:0:7:433:438:268:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
53:3139:185925:7:47:439:554:261:9:g_d0_f49:9:g_d0_f48:.(A4)
53:3140:185930:47:12:60:553:214:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:When ̃δ→0, it follows from hazard-rate dominance thatEj[g]−Ei[g]>0. Note that all elements in A4 are strictly
53:3141:186039:12:0:60:515:202:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f49:positive under hazard rate dominance, which implies that the solution to the ordinary differential equation forh
53:3142:186151:0:8:517:527:210:10:g_d0_f18:9:g_d0_f50:( ̃
53:3143:186154:8:0:521:525:202:9:g_d0_f49:9:g_d0_f49:δ
53:3144:186155:0:8:526:531:210:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
53:3145:186156:8:13:534:554:202:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:must
53:3146:186160:13:0:60:251:189:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f49:be weakly positive everywhere, implying thath
53:3147:186205:0:8:253:263:197:10:g_d0_f18:9:g_d0_f50:( ̃
53:3148:186208:8:0:257:261:189:9:g_d0_f49:9:g_d0_f49:δ
53:3149:186209:0:8:262:267:197:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:)
53:3150:186210:8:28:269:408:189:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:is positive, which shows our claim.
53:3151:186245:28:19:60:217:161:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:B.2  Non-Parametric Test
53:3152:186269:19:12:60:553:142:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:The empirical results presented in Sections 3.3 and 3.4 test the predictions from our model under the assumption
53:3153:186381:12:13:60:553:130:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:that individuals’ beliefs about future house price changes are normally distributed.  We argued that this is a realistic
53:3154:186501:13:13:60:553:117:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:approximation, given the shape of the distribution of actual house price changes. In Section B.1, we also derived non-
53:3155:186619:13:12:60:553:104:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:parametric predictions on the relative mortgage leverage choices of individuals with arbitrary belief distributions.  In
53:3156:186739:12:13:60:553:92:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:particular, we showed that an individual whose belief distribution dominated that of an otherwise identical individual in
53:3157:186860:13:12:60:553:79:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:the sense of hazard rate dominance would choose higher leverage in the housing-as-investment scenario, but lower leverage
53:3158:186981:12:0:60:221:67:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:in the housing-as-consumption scenario.
54:3159:187022:0:12:75:553:729:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:While we are not able to measure the full belief distribution of each individual, we can compare how the leverage
54:3160:187135:12:13:60:553:717:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:choices of individuals vary with the full distribution of the house price experiences of their friends.51Specifically, we
54:3161:187256:13:13:60:553:704:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:conduct pairwise comparisons of the distributions of the house price experiences of all friends (and all out-of-commuting
54:3162:187377:13:12:60:553:691:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:zone friends) of individuals purchasing houses in the same county in the same month, and who borrow from the same
54:3163:187490:12:13:60:553:679:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:lender. We then test whether one distribution dominates the other in a hazard rate dominance sense. We only focus on
54:3164:187606:13:12:60:553:666:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:county-month-lender combinations with at least three mortgage originations: at the average (median) such combination, we
54:3165:187726:12:13:60:553:654:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:have 9.8 (5) mortgage originations. Of all the pairwise comparisons across these originations, we can rank the distribution
54:3166:187849:13:13:60:553:641:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:of all friends’ house price experiences in a hazard rate sense for 3.5% of transaction pairs, and the distributions of out-
54:3167:187972:13:12:60:553:628:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:of-commuting zone friends’ house price experiences for 8.1% of transaction pairs. When comparing the mortgage leverage
54:3168:188090:12:13:60:553:616:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:choices across the individuals with a clear ranking using the experience distribution of all friends, we find that individuals
54:3169:188216:13:12:60:553:603:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:with an experience distribution that is hazard rate dominant will choose 90 basis points lower leverage; this difference is
54:3170:188339:12:13:60:553:591:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:highly statistically significant. When comparing the experience distribution of individuals’ out-of-commuting zone friends,
54:3171:188462:13:13:60:553:578:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:those with a hazard rate dominant distribution choose a 68 basis points lower leverage.  Both of these results provide
54:3172:188580:13:12:60:553:565:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:additional evidence that the housing-as-consumption scenario with a sizable collateral adjustment friction is dominant in
54:3173:188701:12:34:60:96:553:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:the data.
54:3174:188710:34:25:60:231:519:14:g_d0_f7:14:g_d0_f7:C  Additional Evidence
54:3175:188732:25:18:60:219:494:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:C.1  House Price Changes
54:3176:188756:18:13:60:553:476:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Figure A3 shows the distribution of county-year level annual house price changes in the United States since 1993, as
54:3177:188872:13:26:60:141:463:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:measured by Zillow.
54:3178:188891:26:210:187:426:437:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:Figure A3: Distribution of house price changes (1993-)
54:3179:188965:210:8:187:447:227:0:Helvetica:0:Helvetica:-30-20-100102030
54:3180:188981:8:26:236:399:219:0:Helvetica:0:Helvetica:12-Month County-Level House Price Change (%)
54:3181:189025:26:10:60:553:193:8:g_d0_f30:8:g_d0_f6:Note: Figure shows the distribution of county-year level annual house price changes in the United States since 1993, as measured by
54:3182:189156:10:36:60:85:183:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:Zillow.
54:3183:189163:36:19:60:316:147:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:C.2  Downpayments and Expected Default
54:3184:189201:19:12:60:553:128:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:As discussed in Section 4, many personal financial advice websites and blogs discuss the tradeoffs between making larger
54:3185:189321:12:13:60:553:116:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:and smaller downpayments.  Many of these highlight explicitly the tradeoffs that we formally model in Section 1.  For
54:3186:189438:13:13:60:553:103:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:example, the real estate brokerage website Home Point Real Estate describes one of the benefits of high leverage as follows:
54:3187:189562:13:12:68:553:90:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:51This test builds on the survey evidence in Section 3.2, which showed that, on average, the first and second moments of the house
54:3188:189692:12:10:60:553:78:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:price experiences of an individual’s friends affect the corresponding moments of her belief distribution about future house price changes.
54:3189:189830:10:9:60:553:68:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:The assumption underlying the test in this section is that additional moments of the distribution of individuals’ friends’ house price
54:3190:189964:9:0:60:407:59:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:experiences will also shift the corresponding moments of those individuals’ belief distribution.
55:3191:190063:0:12:85:528:729:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:This last reason for putting down a small down payment is kind of twisted, but sadly practical. I doubt we
55:3192:190169:12:13:85:528:717:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:are going to have any down turn in the market soon, and I really doubt it will be like the one we just came
55:3193:190276:13:13:85:528:704:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:through if it does come; but the less you put down the less you lose. Yet, if you are upside down on your home
55:3194:190386:13:16:85:498:691:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:and have to walk away (or lose to foreclosure) the less down payment you put into it the less you lose.
55:3195:190489:16:17:60:364:675:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Reuters (2011) outlines similar reasons for making a smaller downpayment:
55:3196:190562:17:12:85:528:658:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Even if you have the money for a bigger down payment, there can be good reasons to save your cash. Mortgage
55:3197:190669:12:13:85:528:646:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:rates continue to skirt all-time lows: Why not put your money to work for yourself and borrow as much as you
55:3198:190777:13:12:85:528:633:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:can reasonably afford, on a monthly basis, at today’s rates? You can put the money you’re not paying into a
55:3199:190884:12:13:85:528:621:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:down payment to work elsewhere. If home values rise, you will have done your best to leverage a small down
55:3200:190990:13:13:85:528:608:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:payment into bigger equity. If they fall, you’ll have less skin in the game, and that could put more pressure on
55:3201:191102:13:16:85:327:595:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:your banker to improve your loan terms lest you walk away.
55:3202:191160:16:17:60:369:579:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:US News (2017) described the benefits of a smaller downpayment as follows:
55:3203:191234:17:12:85:528:562:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:The last major housing crash scared some people away from low down payments after many homeowners
55:3204:191331:12:13:85:528:550:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:found themselves owing more than their homes were worth when values plummeted. But even a 20 percent
55:3205:191431:13:12:85:528:537:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:down payment won’t protect you against a 50 percent drop in home values. In fact, if you lost your home to
55:3206:191537:12:17:85:476:525:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:foreclosure or did a short sale, you may have lost less money if you made a small down payment.
55:3207:191632:17:12:60:553:508:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:MK Real Estate (2016) describes why making smaller downpayments is the more conservative choice for home buyers
55:3208:191743:12:17:60:231:496:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:worried about potential house price drops.
55:3209:191785:17:13:85:528:479:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:The link between the economy and home prices is the third reason to consider a small down payment. In
55:3210:191886:13:12:85:528:466:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:general, as the U.S. economy improves, home values rise. When the U.S. economy sags, home values sink.
55:3211:191988:12:13:85:528:454:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Buyers with large down payments find themselves over-exposed to economic downturns compared to buyers
55:3212:192089:13:14:85:217:441:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:whose down payments are small.
55:3213:192119:14:13:85:528:427:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Consider the purchase of a $400,000 home and two home buyers, each with different ideas about how to buy a
55:3214:192225:13:12:85:528:414:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:home. One buyer makes a 20% down payment to avoid Private Mortgage Insurance (PMI). The other buyer
55:3215:192324:12:13:85:528:402:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:wants to stay as liquid as possible, and puts down just 3.5%. The first buyer takes $80,000 from the bank and
55:3216:192433:13:15:85:415:389:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:converts it to illiquid home equity. The second buyer puts $14,000 into the home.
55:3217:192514:15:12:85:528:374:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Over the next few years, the economy falters. Our two buyers lose 20% of the value of each of their homes,
55:3218:192620:12:13:85:528:362:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:bringing their values down to $320,000. Neither buyer has any equity left. Our first buyer lost all $80,000 of
55:3219:192730:13:12:85:528:349:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:their money. That money is lost and cannot be recouped except through the housing market’s recovery. The
55:3220:192834:12:13:85:528:337:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:second buyer lost only $14,000. While the second buyer’s home is “underwater,” with more money owed on
55:3221:192936:13:15:85:431:324:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:the home than the home is worth, the bank has the risk of loss and not the borrower.
55:3222:193020:15:12:85:528:309:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Let’s say that both borrowers default and no longer make their payments. Which homeowner will the bank
55:3223:193122:12:13:85:528:297:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:be more likely to foreclose upon? It’s counter-intuitive, but the buyer who made a large down payment is less
55:3224:193231:13:12:85:528:284:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:likely to get relief during a time of crisis and is more likely to face foreclosure. A bank’s losses are limited to
55:3225:193346:12:13:85:528:272:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:when the home is sold at foreclosure. The homeowner’s twenty percent home equity is already gone, so the
55:3226:193450:13:13:85:528:259:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:remaining losses (legal and home prep costs) are easily absorbed by the bank. Foreclosing on an underwater
55:3227:193556:13:12:85:528:246:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:home will lead to great losses. All of the borrower’s money is already lost. The bank will not only have legal
55:3228:193666:12:15:85:392:234:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:and home prep costs, but will also have to write down $66,000 in lost value.
55:3229:193742:15:12:85:528:219:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:Conservative borrowers recognize that risk increases with the size of the down payment. The smaller your down
55:3230:193851:12:29:85:211:207:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:payment, the smaller your risk.
55:3231:193882:29:18:60:292:178:12:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f7:C.3  Downpayment Motivation Survey
55:3232:193916:18:13:60:553:160:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:In Section 4.2, we described results from our Downpayment Motivation Survey. In this Appendix, we provide additional
55:3233:194032:13:13:60:553:147:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:information on the respondent demographics, and more details on how the responses vary with these demographics. For
55:3234:194147:13:12:60:384:134:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:complete disclosure, the full data set is available from the authors upon request.
55:3235:194229:12:13:75:553:122:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:We ran two waves of the survey between September 19, 2017, and September 21, 2017. The survey was designed using
55:3236:194341:13:12:60:553:109:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:the SurveyMonkey survey. Each wave was targeted at approximately 800 homeowners, and no additional filters for the
55:3237:194455:12:13:60:553:97:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:target audience were selected. In each wave, respondents were presented with two different house price growth scenarios,
55:3238:194575:13:13:60:553:84:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:and asked to select one of three different mortgage choices for each house price scenario. In Table A1, we show the changes
55:3239:194698:13:12:60:553:71:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:in the downpayment recommendation between the optimistic and the pessimistic scenario (“Decreased Downpayment”
55:3240:194808:12:0:60:493:59:9:g_d0_f48:9:g_d0_f48:represents individuals who suggested a smaller downpayment in the more pessimistic house price scenario).
